Received 
      25th August 2018
    First published on 11th March 2019AbstractSolar water splitting is a promising approach to transform sunlight into renewable, sustainable and green hydrogen energy. There are three representative ways of transforming solar radiation into molecular hydrogen, which are the photocatalytic (PC), photoelectrochemical (PEC), and photovoltaic–electrolysis (PV–EC) routes. Having the future perspective of green hydrogen economy in mind, this review article discusses devices and systems for solar-to-hydrogen production including comparison of the above solar water splitting systems. The focus is placed on a critical assessment of the key components needed to scale up PEC water splitting systems such as materials efficiency, cost, elemental abundancy, stability, fuel separation, device operability, cell architecture, and techno-economic aspects of the systems. The review follows a stepwise approach and provides (i) a summary of the basic principles and photocatalytic materials employed for PEC water splitting, (ii) an extensive discussion of technologies, procedures, and system designs, and (iii) an introduction to international demonstration projects, and the development of benchmarked devices and large-scale prototype systems. The task of scaling up of laboratory overall water splitting devices to practical systems may be called “an artificial photosynthetic leaf-to-farm challenge”.
Jin Hyun Kim
Jin Hyun Kim received his undergraduate degree from the University of Seoul, Republic of Korea, in 2012 and joined Prof. Jae Sung Lee's research group as an integrated MS + PhD candidate of Pohang University of Science and Technology (POSTECH), Republic of Korea. He received his PhD on the subject of semiconductor photoelectrode development for photoelectrochemical solar energy conversion in 2016. He is now working as a postdoctoral fellow in Jae Sung Lee's group (2012–2018) at Ulsan National Institute of Science and Technology (UNIST) to undertake a project to develop metal oxide-based solar energy harvesting, conversion, and storage.
Dharmesh Hansora
Dharmesh Hansora is pursuing his PhD degree (2017 onwards) under the guidance of Prof. Jae Sung Lee at the School of Energy and Chemical Engineering, UNIST, South Korea. He received his Bachelor's and Master's degree in Chemical Engineering from MS University of Baroda, India. His current research area includes benchmarking of devices and scale-up to large-scale systems for solar water splitting.
Pankaj Sharma
Pankaj Sharma received his PhD degree in Chemistry from Jiwaji University, India, in 2008. He worked as a Senior Lecturer at Lovely Professional University, Punjab, and MM University, Haryana, India, from 2008–2009. In December 2009 he joined Myongji University, Republic of Korea, as a Research Professor. After that he worked at the Korea Institute of Energy Research as a Research Scientist (2010–2012), and then as a Research Professor at Chungnam National University (2012–2017), Republic of Korea. Presently, he is working as a Senior Research Scientist with Prof. Ji-Wook Jang at Ulsan National Institute of Science and Technology, Republic of Korea. His research interests include the synthesis of multifunctional, hybrid and nanomaterials for their applications in energy, environmental and biomedical fields.
Ji-Wook Jang
Ji-Wook Jang studied Chemistry at Pohang University of Science and Technology (POSTECH), Republic of Korea, and obtained his PhD degree in Chemical Engineering from the same university in 2012 under the supervision of Prof. Jae Sung Lee. He continued extensive research on different types of photocatalysts, and photocatalytic systems while working at Boston College, USA, and Institute for Solar Fuels, Germany, under the guidance of Prof. Dunwei Wang, and Prof. Roel van de Krol, respectively. He is presently working as an Assistant Professor at the School of Energy and Chemical Engineering from 2016. His research interest includes the photochemical production of future energy carriers (H2, H2O2, NH3, etc.) and high valued chemicals.
Jae Sung Lee
Jae Sung Lee received his BS degree from Seoul National University in 1975, MS degree from Korea Advanced Institute of Science and Technology in 1977, Korea, and PhD degree from Stanford University in 1984. After a brief tenure at Catalytica Inc. as a research fellow, he returned to his native Korea to become a professor of chemical engineering at Pohang University of Science and Technology. He and his group moved to Ulsan National Institute of Science and Technology in 2013. He has been leading a research group of the “eco-friendly catalysis and energy laboratory”, whose research interests are focused on photocatalysis for energy applications, electrocatalysis for fuel cells, and heterogeneous catalysis of CO2 utilization. In photocatalysis, his research focuses on the development of photocatalytic solid semiconductor materials for solar energy conversion using heterojunction, surface modification, doping and solid solution technologies.
1. Introduction
A fundamental solution to ever-growing concerns on global warming, air pollution, and energy security is to replace the current carbon-rich fossil fuels with renewable and environment-friendly carbon-free or carbon-neutral energy sources.1,2Fig. 1 provides a brief view of the energy reserves available on the earth, which demonstrates the dominant position of solar energy among all renewable and non-renewable energy sources. The total sum of recoverable energy from all the reserves is around 1% of the solar energy supplied to the earth surface by the sun. Moreover, the solar energy (173000 TW)3 supplied to the total surface (land and water) of the earth is nearly 9600 times larger than today's total energy consumption of the world (17.91 TW in 2017).4 Evidently, solar energy is much more than enough to meet all energy needs for the lifetime of the solar system, but the challenge lies in the development of economically viable technologies for its harnessing, storage, and utilization.
 Fig. 1  (a) Comparison of solar energy with other energy resources. Reproduced from ref. 5 with permission from the IEA/SHC Solar Update, copyright 2009. (b) Worldwide mean solar irradiance variation. (Source: https://www.3tier.com/static/ttcms/us/images/support/maps/3tier_solar_irradiance.jpg.) Reproduced with permission from the Vaisala Inc., copyright 2014. (c) Solar thermal power plant. (Source: https://www.solarreserve.com/en/.). Reproduced with permission from the Solar Reserve, copyright 2016. (d) Silicon PV panels and wind turbines. (Source: https://www.shutterstock.com/image-photo/wind-generator-solar-panel-81569431?irgwc=1&utm_medium=Affiliate&utm_campaign=Skimbit%20Ltd.&utm_source=10078&utm_term=.) Reproduced with permission from Shutterstock, copyright 2018. (e) Dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSC) implemented on a building's glass windows. (Source: http://www.elettronews.com/celle-solari-a-sensibilizzante-organico-con-materiali-a-band-gap/.) (f) Biofuel (bioethanol) production from corn. (Source: https://greentumble.com/how-does-biofuel-work/.) (g) Photoelectrochemical water splitting for solar hydrogen production. Reproduced from ref. 6 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. 
There are several technologies available for solar energy utilization – solar thermal (Fig. 1c), photovoltaic (PV) (Fig. 1d and e), and solar fuels (Fig. 1f and g). Today, PV represents the most widely disseminated technology and is one of the fastest growing industries.7 But PV produces electricity, which has an intrinsic difficulty in terms of storage. Considering the intermittent nature of solar energy, converting solar energy into easily storable chemical energy or a fuel has emerged as an ideal option of solar energy utilization. Among the potential solar fuels, hydrogen (H2) generated from solar water splitting (Fig. 1g) has received attention as the most promising fuel especially for powering fuel cell vehicles.
Hydrogen has a higher gravimetric heating value (141.9 MJ kg−1) than most of the conventional fossil fuels (methane 55.5 MJ kg−1, gasoline 47.5 MJ kg−1, diesel 44.8 MJ kg−1, and methanol 20.0 MJ kg−1), and burns with zero emission producing only harmless water. But H2 is industrially produced mostly by the reforming of fossil fuels (most commonly natural gas containing mainly CH4) that emit a large amount of CO2. Hence, there is an urgent need to establish a sustainable, carbon-neutral, and high efficiency H2 production system on an industrial scale at a reasonable cost.8–13 PEC water splitting has a high potential to become such a solar hydrogen production pathway. Extensive techno-economic analysis (TEA) reports have been recently published to evaluate the viability of H2 production by using renewable energy sources including water electrolysis.14,15
There have already been several comprehensive reviews published on sunlight-driven water splitting with an emphasis on materials development,1,16–19 involved physical chemistry,20 and systems.20–25 However, our present review focuses more on technological and engineering aspects to establish a large scale solar hydrogen production system. We discuss three solar-driven water splitting systems based on photocatalytic (PC), photovoltaic–electrochemical (PV–EC) and photoelectrochemical (PEC) cells. We attempt to evaluate the viability of the practical large scale PEC system from technical, economical, and operational points of view and discuss practical cell architectures and conceptual designs. Large-scale demonstration projects that have taken place worldwide are also introduced. Some of the miniature laboratory devices for solar hydrogen production have been called “artificial leaves”.26,27 Then the subject of this review regarding upscaling to practical systems may be termed “artificial plants” or “solar fuel farms”, thus here we call it “an artificial photosynthetic leaf-to-farm challenge”.
2. Photocatalytic and photoelectrochemical water splitting
2.1 Principle and mechanism
Overall water splitting into H2 and O2 molecules is an energetically uphill reaction requiring a standard free energy change (ΔG°) of 237.2 kJ mol−1, or a potential of 1.23 eV per electron. Two electrons are needed to produce a H2 molecule and four holes to produce an O2 molecule according to eqn (1) and (2):Absorption of light: SC + hν (>Eg) → e−cb + h+vb Water oxidation (OER): 2H2O + 4h+vb → O2 + 4H+(1) Proton reduction (HER): 2H + 2e−cb → H2(2) Overall water splitting: 2H2O → 2H2 + O2 ΔG° = 237.2 kJ mol−1(3)
SC indicates a semiconductor which acts as a light absorber. Therefore, to perform water splitting, photons with an energy of >1.23 eV (corresponding to λ < 1010 nm) are needed to generate photo-excited charge carriers and carry out redox reactions at the respective surface sites of a photocatalyst as shown in Fig. 2. In practice, the photons are supplied from sunlight with an energy spectrum shown in Fig. 2a. Thus, a photocatalyst must have a band gap energy (Eg = ECB − EVB) large enough to split water, as well as appropriate band positions to drive the two half reactions by using the photogenerated electrons (ecb−) and holes (hvb+) upon light irradiation. Since both half reactions require significant amounts of kinetic overpotentials, a practical photocatalyst should have a band gap energy in the range of 1.6–2.4 eV to drive the overall water splitting.
 Fig. 2  Solar energy-driven water splitting using a light absorber. If a single light absorber is used, the energy of the photon (hν) should be greater than 1.23 eV. Otherwise, multiple light absorbers are needed to drive the overall water splitting. (a) Spectrum of AM 1.5 G (i.e. 1 sun condition, 100 mW cm−2 power density). (b) Photoelectrolysis of water is an energetically uphill reaction. Reproduced from ref. 18 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2009. (c) Photochemical redox reactions for overall water splitting driven by light of sufficient energy (hν > Eg) to excite electron/hole pairs. E(hν) is the voltage achievable from photons with an energy of hν and Eg is the voltage that the light absorber can achieve using photons. ηloss includes potential loss from the internal resistance (material's own mobility and charge transfer efficiency), quenching of the photon's energy, and overpotential for oxygen and hydrogen evolution reactions (ηOER and ηHER). (d) Technical challenges of photon-driven overall water splitting. 
If the overall water splitting takes place in a PEC cell made of a photoanode (n-type semiconductor) and a photocathode (p-type semiconductor) as shown in Fig. 2c, the process proceeds stepwise: (i) light-induced generation of electron–hole pairs that induce the photocurrents of an n-type semiconductor material as a photoanode, resulting in the formation of electronic charge carriers (excited electrons and holes); (ii) oxidation of water at the photoanode by holes; (iii) transport of H+ ions from the photoanode to the photocathode through the electrolyte, and electron transport through the external circuit to the photocathode; (iv) reduction of hydrogen ions at the photocathode by the electrons. If the semiconductor is immersed in an aqueous electrolyte, band bending near the semiconductor/electrolyte interface provides a driving force for the separation of the photogenerated charge carriers. Band bending can also be generated through the use of a p/n junction or other solid state junctions analogous to a solar cell. The excited holes must reach the surface of the photoanode to drive the oxygen evolution reaction (OER) while the electrons are consumed by the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) at a counter electrode.
A simplified schematic description of the overall water splitting and related technical challenges are summarized in Fig. 2d. Major factors to consider are high solar-to-hydrogen (STH) efficiency, cost competitiveness relative to other H2 production technologies, and stability, which are the major challenges in general for realizing a practical solar H2 production system.28–30 The system is usually composed of 4 components: (i) a photo-electrolysis device, (ii) an electrolyte, (iii) a reactor and (iv) a membrane or separator. The photo-electrolysis device, the core of the system and the main subject of this review, has two major issues of current research – criteria for material selection and efficiency-related factors. The criteria of material selection include the fabrication method, earth abundancy of the elements, cost, and environmental effects. Regarding environmental effects, the solar H2 production does not emit CO2 and is regarded as eco-friendly resembling natural photosynthesis. Extensive reviews and perspectives31,32 are available on strategies and factors to enhance the efficiency, and thus this review will not cover them in detail, but bring the established efficiency and evaluate its viability for practical applications.
2.2 Solar-to-hydrogen conversion efficiency
The performance of PEC water splitting cells is most commonly quantified by the STH conversion efficiency (ηSTH), by which all water splitting systems and devices can be reliably compared.20,33–39 It is determined under standard solar irradiation generated with the Air Mass 1.5 global (AM 1.5 G) filter or one sun (100 mW cm−12) with no applied bias.36,39 The STH efficiency is defined as the amount of chemical (H2) energy produced against the incident solar energy; (4)
The rate of H2 production can be directly measured by gas chromatography or mass spectrometry. Alternatively, it can be determined from the generated photocurrent density (Jsc); (5)where ηF is the Faradaic efficiency of H2 or O2 production, i.e. the efficiency with which charges (electrons/holes) contribute to HER or OER.
There have been a number of studies and reviews20,33,35,40 that report theoretical efficiencies for solar-driven water splitting cells of various configurations. A maximum ηSTH efficiency of ∼31.1% was calculated for PEC water splitting with two light absorbers. The PV–EC system can reach a maximum ηSTH of 28.7% using an efficient bipolar alkaline electrolyzer (73% efficiency) and 2jn PV cells with ∼41% power conversion efficiency (PCE), and an ηSTH of 31.8% using the same efficient electrolyzer and 3jn III–V material-based PV cells with a PCE of ∼43.5%.41 Hisatomi et al.19 showed that the maximum achievable ηSTH is 1.7% for a PC system with a four photon configuration at an apparent quantum yield (AQY) of 1%. However, AQYs of 62, 40 and 30% were needed to achieve an ηSTH of 10% at wavelengths of 600, 700 and 800 nm, respectively.19 The experimental STH efficiencies are discussed in detail in Section 3, but the trend is clear that the highest efficiencies are observed for PV–EC systems, followed by PEC systems, and PC systems show the lowest ηSTH values.
An important analysis was reported by Lewis and coworkers,41 who calculated the maximum efficiency and optimal band gap of light absorbers for PEC tandem systems with three configurations as depicted in Fig. 3; a single light absorber (S2), a side-by-side dual photoabsorber (D4), and a stacked dual photoabsorber (D4).20,40 Previous works reported that the S2 and D4 systems could reach theoretical efficiencies of 29–31% and 40–41%, respectively, thermodynamically without any account of losses.38,40,42 In the S2 system shown, the absorption of a photon (hν) by a semiconductor with a band gap (Eg) creates an electron–hole pair that can be separated by the space charge layer to generate a photo-potential as indicated by the splitting of the Fermi level in Fig. 3a. The maximum theoretical ηSTH for this S2 system is 11.2% for Eg of 2.26 eV. Note that this value falls far short of the thermodynamic limit of ∼30%, highlighting the significant losses associated with reaction overpotentials and the need for better catalysts.
 Fig. 3  (a) Single light absorber-2 electron system (S2) configuration. (b) Maximum theoretical ηSTH of the S2 configuration as a function of the bandgap of the photoabsorber. (c) Dual light absorber (D4) of side-by-side configuration. (d) Maximum theoretical ηSTH of side-by-side D4. (e) Stacked D4 configuration, (f) maximum theoretical ηSTH of stacked D4. Images (b, d and f) were reproduced from ref. 40 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2013. 
The combination of two absorbers increases the available free energy (Δμex). This free energy must be greater than the energy needed for water splitting (1.23 eV) plus the overpotential losses at both anode and cathode, ηOER and ηHER, for the water splitting reaction to occur. Schematics in Fig. 3c and e show the relation of band gaps and photo-voltages for an ideal two light absorber (side-by-side and stacked configurations) tandem photoelectrolysis device in which the anode is the top absorber. Free energy losses in each absorber (Vloss) and catalytic overpotentials for each half-reaction (η) are fundamental loss pathways. The maximum ηSTH is 15.5% for a dual side-by-side light absorber with photoabsorber(s) of the same band gap of 1.59 eV. In comparison, the maximum ηSTH for a dual stacked light absorber system is 22.8% with bottom and top photoabsorbers having Eg of 1.23 eV and 1.84 eV, respectively. These values provide a baseline for reasonable device efficiencies, which can be projected for materials designs within these ranges of band gaps.40
2.3 Types of solar driven water splitting devices
Sustainable solar H2 production systems are based on three water splitting systems19,23,43–46 as schematically depicted in Fig. 4; (i) particulate PC systems, (ii) PEC water splitting cells, and (iii) PV–EC systems. In a particulate PC system, the photocatalyst powders are dispersed in the medium, and thus the charge transfer pathway is significantly shorter (several micrometers) than usual charge collectors in the other systems (sometimes tens of meters). Since both H2 and O2 are generated from each of the PC particles, a separate gas separator is needed. The system is the simplest with only a light absorber and an electrocatalyst. But when the Z-scheme is used, the system becomes D4 and needs a charge mediator adding complexity.
 Fig. 4  Various photo-driven water splitting systems and their charge flows upon light absorption; (a) PC, (b) PEC and (c) PV–EC systems. Typical performance data acquired by these systems are also shown – H2, O2 gas evolution from PC, current matching of the photoanode and photocathode for PEC, and current matching of the PV cell and electrocatalyst (OEC, HEC combined) for the PV–EC system. They also show how to determine the STH efficiency from each system: (a) PC system requires measuring H2 produced per illumination area and time (μmole cm−2 h−1). (b) PEC (photoanode–photocathode) system requires two electrode J–V curves, and the operating point is the photoanode–photocathode cell's short circuit current density, which corresponds to the current density at 0.0 V bias. (c) PV–EC system requires J–V curves of PV and EC, and operating point will be the PV–EC cell's short circuit current density. All data were assumed values for demonstration purposes. For systems like (b) and (c), faradaic efficiency of the reaction is usually confirmed before calculating ηSTH from the current density generated from the system. 
In a PV–EC system, independent power is generated by a PV device, which is connected to electrocatalysts. Thus, the performances of PV and electrocatalysts are of primary importance, while charge transfer between them does not matter. The light absorber is completely out of water, and hence, stability against corrosion in the aqueous medium is of little concern. Also, sizes of PV and EC components can be freely modulated because they have very different balance of system (BOS). The BOS denotes auxiliary components needed for operating the device such as piping, controller, fuel or charge transfer roads, which will be depicted in more detail later. A disadvantage of the PV–EC system is the highest complexity in PV designs, but most of the related technologies are already mature and commercialized.
In a PEC cell, photoelectrodes are connected to charge collectors, and only the light absorber side of the photoelectrode contacts the liquid medium while the charge collector is isolated. Fuel separation can be achieved spatially because H2 and O2 evolve from different electrodes. Band bending is quite obvious relative to PC along the photoanode and photocathode for facile charge separation and transfer. A unique semiconductor–liquid junction (SLJ) is established as described above, but it is also possible to use a semiconductor with a buried junction. For these cases of semiconductor–semiconductor junction (SSJ) (e.g. Pt/TiO2/p/n-Si), metal–insulator–semiconductor (MIS) (e.g. Pt/SiO2/n-Si), or electrocatalyst–metal oxide–semiconductor (e.g. Ni/SnO2/p/n-Si as photoanode),47 their driving force of charge transfer comes from the built-in potential from the interface of solid state materials, not from the semiconductor|electrolyte interface. An extensive review on such principles and examples has been published recently by Bae et al.48
Performance figures of merits for (i) particulate PC systems, (ii) PEC water splitting cells, and (iii) PV–EC systems are discussed in the rest of this paper. For instance, (i) gas evolution for PC, (ii) J–V curves of photoelectrodes and operating point of PEC cells, and (iii) J–V curves of PV cells and electrochemical cells composed of electrocatalyst-based electrodes are presented as typical examples in Fig. 4.
The PEC cells can be operated in different configurations as summarized in Fig. 5. The basic form is either an n-type photoanode–H2 evolution catalytic cathode (HEC) (Fig. 5a), or a p-type photocathode– O2 evolution catalytic anode (OEC) (Fig. 5b). But these single light absorber PEC systems need bias voltage to compensate for the overvoltages and other losses. For spontaneous water splitting, another light absorber is introduced as a photoanode–photocathode tandem cell (Fig. 5c) or a photoanode–photovoltaic (PEC–PV) tandem cell (Fig. 5d) for unbiased operation. Relative to the electrocatalytic water splitting, the PEC cell can reduce the externally supplied power (external bias) or possibly eliminate it completely for overall water splitting. This is accomplished by the photovoltage generated from the photoelectrodes (Vph).49 For instance, the photoanode replaces the OEC anode, while the photocathode replaces the HEC cathode of the electrocatalytic cell to conduct half reactions for water splitting with reduced biases as much as Vph, Fig. 5a and b. If the sum of Vph of the system is larger than E(H2/H2O) + overpotentials of HER/OER (∼1.6 V), the PEC cell can conduct overall water splitting without any applied bias, Fig. 5c and d. Mayer49 arranged Vph achieved from many types of photoelectrodes to show that high Vph (0.8–1.0 V) and Jph (>10 mA cm−2) were achievable mostly by the so called “PV grade, buried junction type” photoelectrodes as discussed later (part 4).
 Fig. 5  Types of PEC cells for water splitting. (a) Photoanode–HEC cathode, (b) photocathode–OEC anode, (c) photoanode–photocathode tandem cell, and (d) photoanode–photovoltaic (PEC–PV) tandem cell for unbiased overall water splitting. Graphs below each figure are examples of J–V curves and operating points achieved from the system. Max ABPE (applied bias to photocurrent efficiency = Jph × (1.23 V – applied bias)/P(100 mW cm−2, 1 sun condition)) indicates maximum power density achievable from the system. Description and denotation were reproduced from ref. 49 with permission from Elsevier B.V., copyright 2017. 
3. Current status of solar water splitting at laboratory scales
Extensive research studies of the past 45 years have observed great advancements in converting sunlight to H2 as a future energy carrier by PC, PEC and PV–EC. In addition to a fundamental understanding of the photon-assisted water splitting reaction with photocatalytic semiconductors, high ηSTH's have been achieved enough to consider practical solar hydrogen production through scale-up of laboratory results. In this section, we summarize and evaluate the past advancements and current status of the photon-driven water splitting at laboratory scales.
3.1 Particulate photocatalysis
The PC water splitting is the simplest solar hydrogen production system with just light and water. As in the schematic image of Fig. 6, the photogenerated charge carriers (h+ and e−) should diffuse rapidly within their lifetimes to respective active sites (HER and OER) on the surface of the semiconductor usually decorated with co-catalysts (HEC and OEC) for high efficiency. Even though the PC system needs the smallest complexity for achieving OWS, there are still many requirements for the light absorber of a proper band gap, band edge potentials, and stability in aqueous medium. In addition, it is difficult to control the charge carriers since there is no applied bias.50 Hence, viable light absorbers for PC systems are extremely limited compared to PEC and PV systems, and their efficiency is relatively much lower as a result of this difficulty.
 Fig. 6  Importance of reaction selectivity for photocatalysis. (a) Energetics of photocatalysis (energetically downhill) and “artificial photosynthesis” (energetically uphill). (b) Selectivity improvement of the photocatalyst via application of a cocatalyst (HEC, OEC) for suppression of unproductive back reactions (HOR, ORR). Reproduced from ref. 51 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. 
It is also necessary to mention the importance of selectivity functions of photocatalysts in PC water splitting systems. Since HER and OER of water splitting occur on the same PC particle in most cases, backward reactions of HOR (hydrogen oxidation reaction) and ORR (oxygen reduction reaction) occur readily, which are energetically more favorable. Osterloh51 illustrated this issue by explaining two possible reaction pathways (Fig. 6) – usual photocatalysis that includes all possible reactions and “artificial photosynthesis” that we specifically want. While photocatalysts can catalyze both energetically uphill (e.g. H2 production) and downhill (consumption of H2 by hole) reactions, application of HEC and OEC can greatly reduce the chance of the backward reactions to occur. The application of these co-catalyst is in fact quite general in photocatalysis and especially application of HEC is often mandatory to have a high efficiency.
Besides ηSTH as the major efficiency, apparent quantum yield (AQY) is a commonly used figure of merit for assessing the efficiency of a PC system.52 The AQY is defined as (6)where n = number of electrons involved in the photocatalytic reaction, R = the production rate of molecules and I = rate of incident photons. Ultimately, AQY of ∼100% till its band gap will grant the theoretical ηSTH corresponding to Eg of the photocatalyst. In reality, AQY at 420 nm is only ∼20%50 even for some of the best photocatalysts known today.
Three selected examples of overall water splitting (OWS) under visible light are introduced in Fig. 7. A pioneering work on successful OWS under visible light (<510 nm) was reported in 2006 by Domen and his coworkers.53,54 Before this work, OWS was achieved only under UV light, and visible light water splitting was observed only for half reactions (HER/OER) in the presence of a sacrificial agent. The solid solution (Ga1−xZnx)(N1−xOx) was synthesized by mixing GaN and ZnO at 850 °C for ∼15 h under NH3 to obtain yellow powders shown in Fig. 7b. A key to the success is core–shell Rh@Cr2O3 particles as a co-catalyst, which promoted HER while suppressing the reverse reaction of water formation. The catalyst showed stability for 35 h in aqueous H2SO4 solution (pH 4.5) and generated nearly 10.8 mmol H2, which represented a marked improvement from its earlier version RuO2/(Ga1−xZnx)(N1−xOx)54 (Table 1). Recently, C3N4 has received much attention as a metal-free conjugated photocatalyst for stable visible light water splitting.50,55 With carbon dots (CDots) as a co-catalyst, a CDots–C3N4 nanocomposite photocatalyst showed quite high ηSTH of 2.0%, a robust stability of 4800 h, and generated H2 at a remarkable rate of 8.4 μmol h−155 (Fig. 7c).
 Fig. 7  Single step PC water splitting systems. (a) Energy diagram. Redrawn from ref. 50 and 52 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2010 and Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2017. (b) Photographic image of (Ga1−xZnx)(N1−xOx) yellowish solid solution obtained by mixing GaN and ZnO. Photocatalytic performance of Rh@Cr2O3-loaded (Ga1−xZnx)(N1−xOx) under visible light (>400 nm) for 35 h with evacuation every 5 h. (red; H2, blue; O2). Reproduced from ref. 54 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2006. (c) UV-vis absorption spectra of C3N4 and CDots–C3N4 nanocomposites, and the time variation H2 and O2 production under >420 nm irradiation using a 300 W Xe lamp for the CDots–C3N4 photocatalyst. Reproduced from ref. 55 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2015. (d) Overall water splitting on a greenish BiVO4 (GBVO4·Bi1−xInxV1−xMoxO4) photocatalyst obtained by dual doping with In and Mo. The overall water splitting under >420 nm irradiation to H2 (solid curves) and O2 (dashed curves) production. The gas evolution test set I, GBVO4; sets II–V, 3 wt% RuO2/GBVO4 and tint-colored line is for bare or 3 wt% RuO2/BiVO4. Reproduced from ref. 56 with permission from the American Association for the National Academy of Sciences, copyright 2015.

BiVO4 is a well-known water oxidation photocatalyst with a modest band gap (2.4 eV). However, the more positive conduction band (CB) position than the hydrogen reduction potential (H+/H2, 0 VRHE) makes it incapable of overall water splitting. Jo et al.56 simultaneously substituted In3+ for Bi3+ and Mo6+ for V5+ in the host lattice of m-BiVO4 to form Bi1−xInxV1−xMoxO4. The material had a slightly larger band gap energy (2.6 eV) showing a greenish color, but more importantly the CB position was pushed up to become more negative than the hydrogen reduction potential. As a result, the material became an overall water splitting photocatalyst (Fig. 7d), showing an apparent quantum yield (AQY) of 3.2% under visible light irradiation, or H2 production of 17 μmol h−1 H2 with RuO2 photo-deposited as a co-catalyst.56
A single photocatalyst for achieving OWS is extremely difficult to find, thus an ingenious strategy is to employ two photocatalysts to increase their overall OWS efficiency, which is often called as the “Z-scheme” for its resemblance to the natural photosystem (PSII/PSI).50 As a particulate D4 PC system, the Z-scheme OWS evolves H2 and O2 separately from two different photocatalysts (Fig. 8a). Thus photogenerated electrons in a hydrogen evolution photocatalyst (HEP) reduce H+ to H2, whereas holes in an oxygen evolution photocatalyst (OEP) oxidize H2O to O2 and H+. The unused electrons and holes in OEP and HEP, respectively, undergo recombination via an aqueous shuttle redox mediator or solid charge conductive materials.50 However, Jia et al.57 reported electron mediator-free Z-scheme PC water splitting by making an efficient BiVO4–Ru/Rh:SrTiO3 composite as depicted in Fig. 8b. The crystallinity and morphology of m-BiVO4 particles played a decisive role for the high activity of the composite photocatalyst, which showed an AQY of 1.6% at 420 nm under AM1.5 solar irradiation.57
 Fig. 8  Two-step photocatalytic water splitting systems. (a) Z-Scheme composed of two photocatalysts decorated with OEC/HEC and connected by an aqueous or solid mediator. Redrawn from ref. 50 and 52 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2010 and Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2017. (b) Z-Scheme of the BiVO4–Ru/Rh:SrTiO3 composite (0.3 g catalyst under 1 sun and irradiation area of 33 cm2). Reproduced from ref. 57 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2013. (c) Z-Scheme of the PtOx/WO3–Pt/Mg:Ta2O6−xNy/TaON water splitting system using an IO3−/I− shuttle redox mediator (75 mg Pt/Mg:Ta2O6−xNy/TaON and 150 mg PtOx/WO3, 150 mL 2 mM NaI solution under 1 sun, irradiation area 15.9 cm2). Reproduced from ref. 58 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2015. (d) Wafer level photochemical OWS on nanowire arrays of Mg:InGaN decorated with Rh/Cr2O3 HEC. Reproduced from ref. 60 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2018. (e) Z-Scheme RuOx/Mo:BiVO4–Au–Ru/La,Rh:SrTiO3 system prepared by the particle transfer method (under 1 sun and two reaction conditions: 288 K, 5 kPa (open symbols), and 331 K, 10 kPa (closed symbols)). The inset shows the generation of gas bubbles on the photocatalytic sheet. Reproduced from ref. 61 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. 
The use of an aqueous redox mediator (IO3−/I−) to form a Z-scheme system by using Pt/MgTa2O6−xNy/TaON and PtOx/WO3 as HEP and OEP was reported by Chen et al.58 This system in Fig. 8c showed 6.8% AQY under irradiation area of 15.9 cm2. Very recently, Qi et al.59 reported a [Fe(CN)6]3−/4− redox-mediated CoOx/BiVO4/Au OEP, and a Rh@Cr2O3/ZrO2-modified TaON HEP with AQY of 10% at 420 nm and STH of 0.5%, which are currently the highest for Z-scheme systems with a liquid charge mediator.
Recent work by Chowdhury et al.60 reported the synthesis of a double-band InGaN nanosheet device, which had the ability to spontaneously induce charge separation and transportation of respective charge carriers to their distinct sites for water redox reactions (Fig. 8d). The InGaN nanosheets were fabricated on Si substrates under a nitrogen atmosphere using the radio frequency plasma-assisted molecular beam epitaxial technique. To make an efficient water splitting photocatalyst, p-type Mg was doped into GaN/InGaN and then Rh@Cr2O3 nanoparticles were deposited as a HEC. The Rh@Cr2O3/Mg:GaN/InGaN photocatalyst evolved H2 (1.62 mmol h−1) and O2 (∼0.81 mmol h−1) at 0 V. To make effective charge transfer across HEP (Ru/La,Rh:SrTiO3) and OEP (RuOx/Mo:BiVO4), Domen and co-workers61 used Au metal as a charge mediator (Fig. 8e). They prepared RuOx/Mo:BiVO4–Ru/La,Rh:SrTiO3 Z-scheme sheets by their unique particle transfer method. They also reported the use of carbon as a charge mediator for the same photocatalysts to observe enhanced STH efficiency (Table 1). The carbon charge mediator significantly improves its scalability owing to lower dependence on noble metals.62
 Fig. 9  PV–electrolysis cell (EC) for overall water splitting. (a) Schematic view. (b) Different types of PV geometry of efficiencies >20%: (A) TOPCon crystalline Si (Fraunhofer). (B) IBC crystalline Si (SunPower). (C) Heterojunction IBC crystalline Si (Panasonic). (D) Multicrystalline Si (Trina Solar). (E) GaAs thin film (Alta Devices). (F) CIGS thin film (ZSW Stuttgart). (G) CdTe thin film (First Solar). (H) Perovskite thin film (KRICT). (c) Theoretical Shockley–Queisser detailed-balance efficiency limit under 1 sun condition as a function of band gap and 75% and 50% of the limit. (c and d) were reproduced from ref. 76 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2016. (d) Experimentally measured overpotentials for HEC (Pt, MoS2, CoP, MoS|P, NiMo) and OEC (IrO2, RuO2, BSCF, NiCeOx/Au, NiFeOx). The overpotentials required to generate 10 mA cm−2 geometric area (grey) or electrochemically active surface area (ECSA) of the catalysts (red) are expressed as bar graphs. Data of EC (OEC and HEC) for budget (green), state of the art (SOA, yellow) and SOA with 10 times greater surface area of SOA OEC/HEC were all assumed values for illustration. Reproduced from ref. 75 with permission from the National Academy of Sciences, copyright 2013. 
3.2 Photovoltaic–electrolysis (PV–EC) system
The technology is straightforward in that electricity generated from a solar cell is used to convert water into H2 and O2. Other renewable energy sources such as wind72 or geothermal energy could also be used. But solar energy is the most important because it is the most abundant source of energy available (Fig. 1a). The major advantage of PV–EC in comparison to PC and PEC is that PV and water electrolysis are already well-established technologies and a high ηSTH of >10% is easily achievable.73 As a result, partial commercialization of the system was already realized (see part 7, Table 4).
In this system, PV cells and the electrolyzer are connected in series making the operating current and voltage the same for the two devices. Therefore, the crossing point between current (J)–voltage (V) curves for the two devices determines the current (Iop) and the voltage (Vop) under operation. Commonly, the PV junction is electrically wired to the HER and OER catalysts in PV–EC wired systems (Fig. 9a), or the catalysts are directly deposited on the PV component often as a thin nano film or as nanoparticles.46,74 The PV device is used only for the light absorber to generate charge carriers (electricity), which are transferred as direct current to the EC cell specifically to the anode (OEC) and cathode (HEC) for OER and HER. These PV cells are kept out from the water electrolyte to avoid the degradation by water. Unassisted water splitting could be realized with a cell voltage of ∼1.8 V, which is totally supplied by the PV cell. Therefore, the key aspects of the PV–EC device are the open-circuit voltage (Voc) of the PV and the overpotentials of water splitting reactions, which are influenced by HEC and OEC.43,75
The primary consideration for a PV–EC system is highly efficient and stable PV cells and low overpotentials of the EC cell. Fig. 9b shows geometry of various solar cells with record power conversion efficiencies (PCE) above 20%; crystalline n-Si with a high quality passivation layer (TOPcon design), interdigital back contact (IBC) design, silicon heterojunction (SHJ), and a passivated emitter, rear cell (PERC) p–n junction design, GaAs high-band gap layers, CIGS with a transparent conducting layer (TCL), CdTe with interfacial layers, and perovskite with hole-selective top layers.76Fig. 9c shows the Shockley–Queisser efficiency limit of a single junction solar cell under standard conditions of 1 sun illumination.76 The maximum efficiency of 33.7% could be achieved for a semiconductor with a single junction with a band gap of 1.34 eV. With multi-junction solar cells, such efficiency can be higher, and to date, the highest efficiency of 38.8% has been achieved with a GaInAs/GaInP/GaAs/AlGaInAs/AlGaInP five-junction tandem geometry under simulated 1.0 sun condition.76 Fundamentally, the PV–EC system's efficiency comes from the power output of PV, thus less than ∼30% of STH is expectable from PV–EC systems under 1.0 sun condition. However, under concentrated sun conditions (e.g. ∼500 sun), the efficiency can go up to 86.8% in theory, while the highest record achieved is currently 46.0%.76 Unlike PC and PEC systems, PV has the advantage of utilizing highly concentrated illumination, which reduces the actual size of PV, which accounts for the majority of cost in PV–EC systems as discussed in depth in part 4.
Besides PV, EC should also come with a high efficiency with a low potential requirement for conducting water splitting by using efficient electrocatalysts.1,77,78 Water splitting involves HER and OER, which require overpotentials to drive a meaningful degree of reaction. As shown in Fig. 9d, at least 1.6 V is needed to conduct overall water splitting even with highly efficient electrocatalysts. This is a lot higher than 1.23 V of theoretical water dissociation energy. Since the overall efficiency of PV–EC depends on both the power conversion efficiency of PV and the over-potential requirement of EC, reducing such a potential requirement is crucial for maximizing ηSTH of PV–EC.
The generalized J–V diagram in Fig. 9e of a directly coupled PV–EC device graphically identifies the power flows relative to the total incident solar power. The plot represents a highly efficient solar cell (ηpv > 30.8%) with an open circuit voltage Voc and a short-circuit current density Jsc; the total plot area is scaled to the incident solar power (100 mW cm−2). The PV–EC system operates at the intersection of the J–V curves of the solar cell and OECs. At the operating current density Jop, power (Jop ·Vop) is dissipated whereas energy is stored at a rate of Jop·μth, where μth is the thermodynamic reaction potential. The difference represents overpotential losses. Coupling loss is defined as the difference between the solar cell maximum output and the power dissipated in the electrochemistry. These losses are represented by the region J > Jop.75
A very good benchmarking study of electrocatalysts for overall water splitting was done by McCrory et al.79 by comparing various electrocatalysts as shown in Fig. 10. In general, HEC is favored in acidic media while OEC is favored in alkaline media with both low overpotentials (∼0.1 V for HER, ∼0.4 V for OER) and longer stability. The work also emphasized the importance of a prolonged (2 h or more) testing time to ensure the validity of an electrocatalyst's capability. Besides traditional precious metal-based electrocatalysts (Pt, Ir, Ru), there are many recent works reporting base metal-based catalysts of comparable or even better activity than that of precious metals.
 Fig. 10  Electrocatalysis for water splitting. (a) Schematic illustration of bias requirement in addition to the theoretical voltage needed for the water splitting reaction (E = 1.23 V), minimum requirement for the actual reaction (overpotential) and bias needed to conduct water splitting with ∼10 mA cm−2 (geometric area of each catalyst). (b) Benchmarking of HEC and OEC in acidic (pH 1.0, 1.0 M H2SO4) and basic (pH 13.6, 1.0 M NaOH) electrolytes. Overpotential needed to generate 10 mA cm−2 was used as the figure of merit. Reproduced from ref. 79 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. (c) J–V curves of HER for CoPS films on graphite, CoPS NWs on graphite, and CoPS NPls on carbon fibre paper, and Pt wire. Reproduced from ref. 82 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2015. (d) iR-corrected polarization curves of NiFe:LDH/Fe foam in 1 and 10 M KOH. Reproduced from ref. 81 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2018. 

Fig. 10c shows the electrochemical properties of earth abundant nanostructured cobalt phosphosulphide (CoPS) electrodes and their HER catalytic performance in 0.5 M H2SO4. The CoPS electrodes made as films, wires, and plates achieved geometric current densities of −10 mA cm−2 at very low overpotentials of −128 mV, −61 mV and −48 mV, respectively, versus RHE. As compared to CoPS films and CoPS nanowires, CoPS nanoplates were more stable for >35 h without any degradation in 0.5 M H2SO4.75 Active OECs are generally metal and metal oxides,78 and IrO2 is the most common practical OER catalyst in acids, which is explained on the basis of an optimum binding energy of the reaction intermediates. Earth abundant OEC materials like NiFe-LDH are active and cost effective.78,80 Corrosion engineering employed to form NiFe-LDH on iron substrates resulted in an electrocatalyst with the best stability (6000 h) in bases ever demonstrated for non-noble metal catalysts.81 The result shows the feasibility of replacing the noble metal electrocatalysts in bases. Fig. 10d shows the iR-corrected polarization curves of NiFe-LDH measured in 1 M and 10 M KOH.81 The OEC can afford current densities of 500 and 1000 mA cm−2 at 300 and 400 mV in 1 M KOH, while in 10 M KOH it requires only 257 and 280 mV to generate the same current densities. The performance is close to that of practical water–alkali electrolyzers and hence meets the requirement of practical large-scale water splitting technology.
Chang et al.83 discussed the design principles for combining PV cells, electrolyzers, and electrocatalysts. All these components can be integrated into solar water splitting devices while minimizing their power loss during the conversion of electrical energy into fuel. The effects of exchange current density, Tafel slope, system resistance, electrode area, light intensity, and PCE of PV are the governing factors. More importantly, an effective combination of independent PV and EC cells is the key for improving the STH efficiency of the whole PV–EC system. Modelling of the PV–EC device showed a maximum STH conversion efficiency of up to 30.8% using a single light absorber.75 Different solar cells such as c-Si, CIGS, GaAs, CdTe and a-Si could reach theoretical limiting STH efficiencies of 26.9, 26.1, 19.7, 19.2 and 26.8%, respectively. The maximum ηSTH for a tandem absorber + electrocatalyst configuration was calculated to be 28.7% with band gap combination of 1.60 eV/0.95 eV and electrolyzer efficiency of 73%. However, ηSTH can be increased up to 31.8% using a 3jn PV cell, and to 62% with an efficient 4jn PV cell.41,83 These have to be considered for practical solar hydrogen production by the PV–EC technology.
A crystalline silicon (c-Si) 3jn solar cell was integrated with NiMoZn (as cathode) and Ni-Bi (as anode) of an EC cell (Fig. 11a), which showed ηSTH greater than 10%.84 Connecting OEC to the p-type terminal and HEC to the n-type terminal of the PV cell, the current–voltage of the PV–EC system has to be equal regardless of the mode of implementation, i.e. wired or wireless. The PV–EC cell was composed of multiple series buried junctions of c-Si solar cell–Ni-Bi (OEC)/NiMoZn (HEC) for both wired and wireless systems. The point of intersection determines Jop and ηSTH of the PV–EC system. The ηSTH was estimated as 2.8% for the 3-cell module and 10% for the 4-cell module of the c-Si solar cell. The maximum current density for H2O oxidation at the anode was 8.13 mA cm−2 with an ηSTH of 9.8%. This approach allows independent modular optimization, after which the PV cell, anode and cathode could be integrated into a monolithic design.84 A typical wired Si PV – membrane electrode assembly (MEA) electrolyzer is shown in Fig. 11b.
 Fig. 11  PV–EC systems and their water splitting performance. (a) c-Si solar cell–Ni-Bi–NiMoZn. J–V curves of PV modules of three (dashed) and four (solid) single junction c-Si solar cells under simulated 1 sun and EC (dashed line, the ideal curve for Ni-Bi/NiMoZn catalysts; solid line, Ni-Bi/NiMoZn electrodes in a two-electrode cell (0.5 M KBi/0.5 M K2SO4, pH 9.2)). The point of intersection represents Jop and ηSTH of the PV–EC system. Reproduced from ref. 84 with permission from the National Academy of Sciences, copyright 2014. (b) A 3 series connected c-Si/a-Si heterojunction (SHJ) solar cell – membrane electrode assembly (MEA) electrolyzer; operating point of the system under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 89 with permission from the Electrochemical Society, copyright 2016. (c) Dye-sensitized solar cell (DSSC)-Pt system. J–V curve of DSSC-Pt in 1 M NaOH under 1 sun irradiation. ABPE and ηSTH at zero voltage. Reproduced from ref. 85 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2016. (d) A triple-junction organic photovoltaic (OPV) cell architecture connected to Pt–Ni electrocatalysts. The PCEs and ηSTH at various operating voltages under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 86 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2016. (e) Perovskite solar cell coupled with NiFe-LDH/Ni. J–V curves of the perovskite tandem cell and the NiFe-LDH/Ni foam electrodes in a two-electrode configuration under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 87 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2014. (f) A triple-junction solar cell (InGaP/GaAs/GaInAsSb), and two PEM electrolyzers (Ir–Pt/C) connected in series with ηSTH over 30%. J–V curves at the beginning-of-operation (BOO) and end-of operation (EOO) for the triple-junction solar cell (under concentrated 42 suns) and the dual-electrolyser. Reproduced from ref. 88 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. 

Fig. 11c shows a system of a parallel tandem DSSC with a Pt electrode, which demonstrates an ABPE of 7.4% and ηSTH of 5.75% measured with applied biases of 0.248 V and 0 V, respectively, under 1 sun illumination for 5 h.85Fig. 11d shows PBDTT:PCBM-PEDOT:PSS as an organic photovoltaic (OPV) and Pt wire–Ni foam as cathode–anode, which shows PCE and ηSTH at various operating voltages of the PV–EC system measured in 1 M NaOH.86 An EC cell driven by a highly efficient homo tandem 3jn polymer solar cell demonstrated an ηSTH of 6.1% at 1.5 V. Fig. 11e shows a perovskite (CH3NH3PbI3) solar cell–NiFe-LDH (as both OEC and HEC) system for overall water splitting, where low-cost bifunctional earth-abundant NiFe-LDH catalysts were loaded on Ni foam. Perovskite solar cells were arranged side-by-side and connected through wires to catalysts immersed in water.87 The operating point at an ηSTH of 12.3% was demonstrated in a stable operation for 10 h in 1 M NaOH. This ηSTH is equivalent to 78.3% of the maximum power of the PV cell.87
Recently, Jia et al.88 achieved the highest ever ηSTH (>30%) with a PV–EC system of InGaP/GaAs/GaInNAsSb 3jn solar cell connected to 2 polymer electrolyte membrane (PEM) electrolyzers in series with Pt–Ir as the cathode–anode coated on a Nafion membrane under 42 sun (Fig. 11f). The PEM electrolyzer is composed of a thin solid state ion conducting membrane (e.g. Nafion) instead of an aqueous solution between two electrodes with precious metal electrocatalysts such as Ir, Ru, and Pt. Its operating conditions (20–200 °C, 200–2000 psi) are harsher than those of well-established alkaline electrolyzers (20–80 °C, <500 psi) using cheaper base metal electrocatalysts like Ni, Co, and Fe.72,73 Water flows in the anode compartment of the first electrolyzer and then into the anode compartment of the second electrolyzer, and in the same way, H2 flows from the cathode of the first electrolyzer to the cathode of the second electrolyzer. The current–voltage characteristics of the PV–EC system show beginning of operation (BOO) and end of operation (EOO). This efficient system was stable up to ∼48 h without interruption in pure water. The current–voltage characteristics of both solar cell and PEM electrolyzers were the same before and after 48 h of operation.
Efficient non-precious metal electrocatalysts have been extensively explored to replace expensive noble metal catalysts like Pt, IrO2, and RuO2 as OEC and HEC for water splitting. For example, NiFe-LDH (OEC) is efficient90 as well as durable up to 6000 h (8 months)81 as discussed earlier. A transition metal hexacyanometallate (MHCM)–NiMoS catalyst as anode–cathode powered by a commercial III–V (InGaP/GaAs/Ge) 3jn solar cell demonstrated an ηSTH of 17.9% and stability for 100 h at neutral pH.91 A wireless and integrated PV–EC device (a-Si:H/c-Si:H 1jn and 2jn solar cell–Ni–Mo or Ni–Co) showed an ηSTH of 3.9% under bias free operation of 40 h in 1 M KOH. The concept was successfully scaled-up up to 64 cm2 device area.92 A miniature device concept was presented by a simple, small and stand-alone bias-free device. A small membraneless solar driven water splitting device (1.35 cm2) consisting of series-connected spherical Si solar cells and non-noble metal electrocatalysts (NiMo–NiFe as cathode–anode) showed ηSTH values of 7.4% and 6.4% using 3-series and 4-series PV solar cells, respectively. This stand-alone and easily-scalable modular PV–electrolyzer device performed a stable operation for 2 h in 0.5 M KOH.93 A PV–electrolysis system composed of a c-Si/a-Si:H heterojunction solar cell (1.9 cm2 for single cell, and 3 series connected for total illumination area 3p c-Si, ∼5.7 cm2) and an MEA electrolyzer with microstructured Ni foam-based earth abundant electrocatalysts showed ηSTH up to 14.2% (Fig. 11b).89 This system containing a Pt–IrOx catalyst layer on a Nafion membrane also demonstrated stable ηSTH of 14.2–14.5% for >100 h, and also comparable ηSTH with Ni–Ni foam electrocatalysts for an alkaline electrolyzer in 1 M KOH. Just usual Ni foam was in fact efficient enough for achieving a high ηSTH. Bonke et al.94 achieved an ηSTH of 22.4% using Ni–Ni foam connected to a GaInP/GaAs/Ge 3jn solar cell under simulated 1.0 sun condition. They also showed the size dependence of Ni foam (1–20 cm2) and evaluated stability and efficiency in various electrolytes with pH ranging from 7.0–14.0, showing that pH 14.0 was the best condition.
In addition to these examples, Table 2 summarizes recently reported PV–EC systems that show ηSTH values of 10–30% under 1 sun illumination under different pH conditions. The ηSTH values have been improved over time and there is still room for further improvement by interface engineering and optimization of each compartment.107
