3.3 Photoelectrochemical (PEC) system
As mentioned earlier, the PEC cell is located between PC and PV–EC in its complexity, choice of material (light absorber), and obtained overall efficiency. In this section, a number of examples of photoanodes and photocathodes are discussed for PEC water splitting cells. Usually photoelectrodes have the following configuration:
Photoanode = OEC/protection layer/dopant:light absorber/substrate
Photocathode = HEC/protection layer/dopant:light absorber/substrate
The substrates are commonly transparent conductive oxides on glass, i.e. fluorine-doped tin oxide (FTO) or indium tin oxide (ITO). However, metal-based substrates are used in some cases, which are specified as appropriate. Since there are various types of PEC cells, strategies used to develop them are different from each other.102 Therefore, we would like to address only common general backgrounds behind their performance.

3.3.1. Photoanodes. 
Various n-type metal oxide semiconductors (TiO2, WO3, Fe2O3, BiVO4, etc.) are candidates for practical photoanode owing to their inherent stability in water, which requires lower complexity and less stringent protection.16,31,103 However, these material classes have two major challenges. First, they have relatively large band gaps with poor optical properties. For instance, BiVO4 is currently the best oxide-based photoanode material, but has a relatively large band gap of 2.4 eV,16 while the ideal band gap for the D4 tandem cell's photoanode is 1.84 eV.40 In addition, the absorption properties of the majority of metal oxides are rather poor because of their pronounced indirect bandgap characteristics in comparison with PV grade materials. The second challenge is their poor conductivity, low charge carrier transfer efficiency and small mobility. The charge mobility of metal oxides is only 1.0–0.0001 cm2 V−1 s−1, while that of typical PV grade materials is 100–10000 cm2 V−1 s−1.16,102 A number of strategies have been employed to address these two issues; developing advanced synthesis methods and improving charge carrier dynamics by (i) intrinsic/extrinsic doping, (ii) fabricating a heterojunction of an electron transfer layer (ETL) or hole transfer layer (HTL), (iii) co-catalyst loading (OEC, HEC), (iv) nano-structuring, and (v) surface passivation.103,104 Herein, we introduce some of the highest performing metal oxide semiconductors to assess their viability for practical applications.
TiO2 (3.0–3.2 eV) and WO3 (2.6–2.8 eV) have good electrical properties and stability, but they show moderate PEC performance (Jph of 1–3 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE),16 because of their too large band gaps. Hence here we cite only a few examples for these materials that nicely demonstrated the modification strategies mentioned above. A nanoplate array film of WO3 exhibited unprecedented high PEC performance (3.7 mA cm−2 @ 1.23 VRHE) without adding any cocatalyst or sacrificial reagent.105 Co-doping of tungsten and carbon into TiO2 nanowires, W, C:TiO2 NWs, showed water splitting performance (1.81 mA cm−2 @ 1.23 VRHE) due to the synergistic effects of co-doping. No obvious degradation of the photocurrent was observed during 30 min experimental time interval, and even after over 6 months of storage in a strong base (2 M KOH) solution, indicating excellent chemical stability.106
Thus the major focus has been moved towards narrower band gap materials like BiVO4 and Fe2O3. Selected examples of state-of-the-art photoanodes based on BiVO4 and Fe2O3 are presented in Fig. 12. Among metal oxide-based photoanodes, most remarkable progress has been made in the performance of BiVO4 since the discovery of its photocatalytic water oxidation capability by Kudo et al.107 Its photoelectrochemical and charge transfer properties are not necessarily favorable as an efficient photoanode material. Hence, multiple modification strategies have been addressed including morphology control, formation of composite structures, composition tuning, and coupling with oxygen evolution catalysts.104 In particular, in situ metal organic deposition (MOD), electron transport layers (ETLs) as well as scaffolds such as WO3, and electrocatalysts such as cobalt phosphate (Co-Pi), FeOOH and NiFeOx have contributed greatly to the development of this photoanode material. The current state-of-the-art, solution-processed BiVO4 photoanodes demonstrated by Choi and coworkers108 (double OEC NiOOH/FeOOH109 loaded, nanoporous, N2 gas treated BiVO4) routinely record a photocurrent density of ∼5.0 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE, ABPE of ∼2.1% and high stability of ∼30 h.
 Fig. 12  State-of-the-art BiVO4 and Fe2O3 photoanodes for PEC water splitting. (a) A PEC cell with NiOOH/FeOOH coated BiVO4 photoanode, fabricated by thermal conversion of BiOI films, from ref. 122 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. The J–V curve for the FeOOH/NiOOH/BiVO4 photoanode in the presence of V5+ in KBi buffer (pH 9.5). Reproduced from ref. 110 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. (b) Structure and energy band diagram of a W:BiVO4/WO3 core/shell NW photoanode, and its PEC performance in 0.5 M KPi electrolyte buffered at pH 8. Reproduced from ref. 112 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2014. (c) The fabrication of the conductive 3D nanocone array substrate and BiVO4 deposition. The 60° tilted SEM image of the final F:SnO2/Pt/SiO2 nanocone arrays, and the cross section image of the uniformly grown Mo:BiVO4 on the 3D nanocone arrays. J–V curves of the Mo:BiVO4 nanocone in 0.5 M Na2SO3-containing KPi buffer solution, and NiOOH/FeOOH/Mo:BiVO4 nanocone photoanode in KPi buffer solution. Reproduced from ref. 113 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2016. (d) Co-Pi loaded Si-doped α-Fe2O3 photoanode. Inset image; the tree shaped morphology of the nanostructured Co-Pi/Si:α-Fe2O3 photoanode. Reproduced from ref. 118 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2011. (e) Synthesis of TiO2/Ti:α-Fe2O3 3D branched nanorod arrays (BNRs) on TiO2/FTO substrates. Reproduced from ref. 119 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2017. (f) Modified Fe2O3/FTO photoanodes prepared by PADD and their J–V curves in 1 M KOH, under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 6 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. 
In a PEC cell with a NiOOH/FeOOH/N2 gas-treated BiVO4 photoanode (Fig. 12a),110 Lee et al. demonstrated that the use of a V5+-saturated electrolyte (KB + V) inhibited the photooxidation-coupled dissolution of BiVO4, thereby effectively suppressing the anodic photocorrosion of BiVO4. The V5+ species in the solution can also be incorporated into the FeOOH/NiOOH OEC layer present on the BiVO4 surface during water oxidation to further enhance water-oxidation kinetics. The J–V plots measured in the KB and KB + V electrolytes shows a similar trend, suggesting that the presence of H2VO4−/HVO42− species does not interfere with the interfacial charge transfer of holes from BiVO4 to water. The J–t curve shows the photostability of NiOOH/FeOOH/BiVO4 at 0.6 VRHE in KB + V electrolyte over 500 h. The formation of a BiVO4/WO3 heterojunction111 turned out to be one of the most efficient strategies to obtain highly active BiVO4-based photoanodes for improved bulk and surface charge transfer efficiency. In Fig. 12b, a WO3@W:BiVO4 core@shell nanowire (NW) photoanode was fabricated by coating a thin layer of BiVO4 on a vertical array of electrically more conductive WO3 nanowires. Thus WO3 NWs acted as an electron conductor while W:BiVO4 acted as the light absorber. A combination of both achieved high light absorption and a photocurrent of 3.1 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE under 1 sun. This photoanode was stable for 1 h without degradation in 0.5 M KPi at pH 7.112 Light management by nanostructured substrates can also be achieved by using a scaffold such as nanocone or pillar-type TCO. Nanoporous Mo:BiVO4 on top of light trapping nanocone architectures was fabricated by a two-step process113 as shown in Fig. 12c. In the first step, SiO2 nanoparticles of controlled diameters were densely arranged by the Langmuir–Blodgett method and 3D nanocones were formed by a reactive ion etching process. Next, nanoporous Mo:BiVO4 was formed on the nanocone arrays by a sol–gel method. The optimized Mo:BiVO4 nanocone exhibited a very high photocurrent density of 6.05 mA cm−2 at 1.23 V RHE in phosphate buffer solution containing 0.5 M sodium sulfite (Na2SO3) as the hole scavenger. The NiOOH/FeOOH/Mo:BiVO4 nanocone photoanode showed photocurrents approaching this value under water oxidation conditions and a good stability for 5 h. Kuang et al.114 also demonstrated yet one of the longest stability values of BiVO4 (∼1100 h) at 0.4 VRHE by adding Ni cations for regenerative healing of the surface of NiFeOx OEC.
Hematite (Fe2O3) has been one of the most popular materials of choice with BiVO4 for metal oxide-based photoanodes. With a band gap of 2.1 eV (indirect), its theoretical ηSTH is as high as ∼15%. Iron is the 4th most abundant element on the earth and has inherent stability in alkaline water. These attributes would make it an excellent candidate for a practical photoanode for PEC water splitting cells if its poor optical and electrical properties are overcome. In particular, it has an extremely short hole diffusion length of 2–4 nm, which is much shorter than those of BiVO4 (∼70 nm) and WO3 (∼500 nm).24 However, steady improvement in PEC performance of hematite has been made over the past few decades through extensive research led by the research groups of Grätzel and Sivula,23,115–117 making it a highly performing metal oxide photoanode material, yet still having much room for further improvement.32

Fig. 12d shows that photoelectrodeposition of Co-Pi on Fe2O3 photoanodes improves the photocurrent density to 2.8 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE under 1 sun at pH 13.6.118 Both Fe2O3 and Co-Pi/Fe2O3 films showed comparable cathodic shifts of their photocurrent onset potentials. The FeOOH/TiO2/Ti:Fe2O3 nanorods (NR) and branched nanorods (BNR) were fabricated by the hydrothermal method, annealing, doping, interlayer deposition and cocatalyst loading as shown in Fig. 12e.119 The J–V curves of TiO2/Ti:Fe2O3 NR, and TiO2/Ti:Fe2O3 BNR and FeOOH/TiO2/Ti:Fe2O3 BNR photoanodes exhibit photocurrent densities of 1.5, 2.5, and 3.1 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE, respectively, in 1 M KOH under 1 sun illumination. The FeOOH/TiO2/Ti:Fe2O3 BNR also showed noticeable stability by maintaining 95% of the performance after 50 h of operation. Platinum-doped and Co-Pi surface modified wormlike hematite photoanodes are also promising. Stable and efficient hematite-based photoanodes were prepared by a solution method followed by high temperature (800 °C) annealing, and the resulting Co-Pi/Pt:Fe2O3 photoanode showed a record performance of 4.32 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE under 1 sun irradiation.120 Multi-dimensional nano/micro-architectures of Fe2O3 nanoflakes decorated by CoOx OEC showed high photoactivity (3.25 mA cm−2 @ 1.23 VRHE) as a photoanode for water oxidation under 1 sun.121 More recently, Kim et al.6 reported polymer-assisted decomposition deposition (PADD) to make nanoporous (particle size: ∼20 nm) Fe2O3, and Ti doping and post H2 treatment (by decomposition of NaBH4) were applied to increase the bulk charge separation efficiency (Fig. 12f). Eventually, a Ni2FeOxHy/TiO2 overlayer/H2-treated Ti:Fe2O3 photoanode showed a photocurrent density of ∼4.5 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE in 1.0 M KOH, which is yet the highest Jph achieved for a Fe2O3 photoanode. Since Fe2O3 has a theoretical Jph of 12.4 mA cm−2, there is still more than 2 fold room for improvement even though the progress is extremely slow.
An interesting material class of semi-stable nitrides and oxynitrides have been extensively studied by Domen and co-workers such as TaON, BaTaO2N, Ta3N5, LaTaON2, LaTiO2N, SrTaO2N, CaTaO2N, Li2LaTaO6N, and CaLaTiON as shown in Fig. 13a. The hybridization of O 1s and N 1s for the VB makes its potential rather reduced, thus narrowing its band gap and making it more suitable for visible light harvesting.52,54 Even though their efforts have been devoted mostly to PC applications (Fig. 7, 8 and Table 1), these nitride materials also turned out to be pretty good photoanodes. To acquire long term stability, surface protection is absolutely necessary as recently demonstrated for Ta3N5.123,124 As in Fig. 13b, anodically grown Ta2O5 NR on Ta metal foil showed a uniform green color originating from light interference in the nanostructured film, which transformed into red Ba:Ta2N5 upon nitridation.125 The vertically aligned Ba:Ta3N5 NR array had a uniform diameter and length of ∼60 nm and ∼600 nm, respectively. The Co-Pi/Ba:Ta3N5 NR photoanode showed a cathodic shift of 50 mV relative to the undoped Co-Pi/Ta3N5 NR photoanode and a very high photocurrent density of 6.7 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE.
 Fig. 13  Oxynitride photoanodes for PEC water splitting. (a) Photocatalytic oxynitrides and nitrides (with courtesy of Domen's group). (b) The transformation of green Ta2O5 film into red Ba-doped Ta3N5. Vertically aligned Ba:Ta3N5 NR arrays. J–V curves for Co-Pi/Ta3N5 and Co-Pi/Ba:Ta3N5 NR photoanodes under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 125 and 130 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2013. (c) The three-electrode cell with an InGaN photoanode for PEC water oxidation. The J–V curve of InGaN and IrO2/InGaN photoanodes in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 126 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. (d) Water oxidation on a CoOx/BaTaO2N/Ta/Ti photoelectrode in 0.2 M KPi solution of pH 13 under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 127 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. (e) The molecular catalyst integrated Ta3N5 photoanode. J–V curves of the WOC complex 2/WOC complex 1/Ni(OH)x/FeOOH/TiOx/Ta3N5 photoanode (1 sun, 1 M NaOH). Reproduced from ref. 128 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2016. 
An InGaN NW photoanode was prepared on a large-area wafer as in Fig. 13c.126 In PEC water oxidation in 0.5 M H2SO4 under 1 sun illumination, IrOx/InGaN NWs produced a photocurrent density of 10.9 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE. However, a photocurrent density of 21.2 mA cm−2 at 1.23 V RHE was obtained in the presence of a hole scavenger, which is near the theoretical limit for 1.7 eV InGaN. But real water splitting without the scavenger was not realized due to stability issues under water oxidation conditions. The co-catalyst IrOx loading shifted the photocurrent onset potential by 500 mV. A BaTaO2N photoanode was prepared by the particle transfer method in Fig. 13d.127 The Co/BaTaO2N photoanode produced ∼4.2 mA cm−2 at 1.2 VRHE and showed stability for 6 h in 0.2 M KPi solution at pH 13. Ta3N5 has attracted great attention as the best nitride-based photoanode material. As shown in Fig. 13e, an integrated Ta3N5 photoanode (Ni(OH)2/FeOOH/TiO2/Ta3N5) shows a record photocurrent of 12.1 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE approaching the theoretical limit (12.9 mA cm−2) for the material, which represents the highest record for non-PV grade materials. In this efficient photoanode, Ni(OH)x/FeOOH is a hole storage layer that mediates interfacial charge transfer from Ta3N5 to coupled molecular catalysts (Co-oxycubane and Ir complex) for water oxidation, and the TiOx blocking layer reduces the surface electron–hole recombination.128 Efficient perovskite-type BaNbO2N photoanodes showed solar-driven water oxidation photoactivity of ∼5.2 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE absorbing visible light up to 740 nm.123 Nanostructured core@shell (Si@Ta3N5) heterojunction photoanodes along with CoTiOx as OEC (CoTiOx/Si@Ta3N5) showed water oxidation performance of 2.6 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE by overcoming the charge transport limitations of Ta3N5. Photoanodes (NiOx/CoTiOx/Si@Ta3N5) showed stability up to 24 h after coating with NiOx.129
Another class of photoanodes is the buried junction, PV-grade semiconductors composed of elements like Si, Ga, In, Ge, Zn, Cd, Cu, P, S, Se, As, Sb, Te, etc.102 which are protected against corrosion. This class of light absorbers like c-Si, CdTe, GaAs, GaP, a-Si, etc.131 have excellent bulk charge transfer efficiency. For instance, the typical oxide semiconductor Fe2O3 has a very low hole mobility (0.0001 cm2 V−1 s−1), while PV grade InP has an extremely high hole mobility of 5400 cm2 V−1 s−1.102,132 But their instability in water requires a sophisticated protection layer for PEC application in water.48,102
As shown in Fig. 14a, ALD coatings (44 nm) of TiO2 along with NiO electrocatalysts on n–p+-Si photoanodes showed a stable photocurrent density of 34.7 ± 1.7 mA cm−2 at 0.4 VSCE in water oxidation under 1.25 sun in a continuous operation for 100 h in 1 M KOH. Instead of corroding the anode, the photogenerated holes are conducted through the TiO2 layer to Ni electrocatalysts, where the holes are used to oxidize water.131 In Fig. 14b, ultrathin Ni (2 nm) and TiO2 (118 nm)-coated np+-GaAs and GaP photoanodes showed photovoltages of 0.89 and 0.59 V, and water oxidation photocurrent densities of 14.3 and 3.4 mA cm−2 at 1.2 VRHE, respectively, under 1 sun.131 CdS/CdTe forms a p–n junction that serves as a photoanode for OER with a photocurrent density of 3.8 mA cm−2 at 1.2 VRHE. The CdTe-based photoanode was layered with Co(OH)x/Ti/MoOx/CdTe/CdS. A biphotoelectrode PEC cell composed of a CdTe-based photoanode as well as a photocathode exhibited a STH conversion efficiency of 0.22% without an external voltage.133
 Fig. 14  Photoanodes with PV grade materials with buried junctions. (a) Protected semiconductors by TiO2 deposited by ALD. (b) J–V curves of 2 nm Ni/118 nm TiO2-coated np+-GaAs and n-GaP photoelectrodes under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 131 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science (AAAS), copyright 2014. (c) Chronoamperometry and (d) J–V curve of NiOx/TiO2/HTJ-Si, NiOx/n-CdTe and NiOx/a-Si:H photoanodes. Reproduced from ref. 134 with permission from the American Association for the National Academy of Sciences, copyright 2015. (e) J–V curves of NiOx/CoOx/SiOx/n-Si and NiOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanodes in 1 M KOH. (f) Chronoamperometry of NiOx/CoOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanodes biased at 1.63 V RHE under 1 sun. The inset shows the TEM image. Reproduced from ref. 135 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. 

Fig. 14c shows NiOx/TiO2/HTJ-Si (HTJ = heterojunction), NiOx/n-CdTe and NiOx/a-Si:H based photoanodes, with reactively sputtered NiOx films that provide transparent, anti-reflective, electrically conductive, and chemically stable coatings as well as a highly active electrocatalyst.134 The NiOx films stabilized all of these self-passivating, high-efficiency semiconducting photoanodes (NiOx/TiO2/HTJ-Si and NiOx/a-Si:H) for >100 h of stability, while NiOx/n-CdTe showed 1000 h stability at 1.73 VRHE in 1 M KOH.134Fig. 14d shows the cyclic voltammetry performance (J–V curves) under 1 sun, where the photocurrent onset potentials were −70 mV and −116 mV for photocurrent densities of 5.2 and 3.3 mA cm−2 for NiOx/a-Si:H and NiOx/n-CdTe photoanodes, respectively. The Ni(II/III) redox peaks in general increased in current and separated in potential during the stability tests, suggesting an increment in catalytically active sites, loss of reversibility, and/or an increase in resistance. An ultrathin (2 nm) film of CoOx on an n-Si photoanode and multifunctional coating of NiOx provided a stable photoanode for water oxidation at a photocurrent density of 27.7 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE in 1 M KOH under 1 sun.

Fig. 14e presents the J–V curves of NiOx/CoOx/SiOx/n-Si and NiOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanodes in 1 M KOH under 1 sun illumination.135 The presence of the interfacial CoOx layer between SiOx/n-Si and NiOx resulted in 165 mV shift in the photocurrent-onset potential. The photocurrent density for the NiOx/CoOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanode structure was 27.7 mA cm−2, while the NiOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanode showed only 6.3 mA cm−2. The chronoamperometry of NiOx/CoOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanodes in 1 M KOH biased at 1.63 VRHE under 1 sun recorded a current density of 30 ± 2 mA cm−2 for 1700 h of continuous operation (Fig. 14f). The TEM image of the NiOx/CoOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanode shows ∼2 nm thin SiOx layer on the surface of crystalline Si and 2–3 nm thin layer of CoOx.135


3.3.2. Photocathodes. 
Water splitting can also be achieved with a p-type semiconductor as a photocathode for electron transfer toward the electrolyte. The ideal band gap for the D4 tandem cell is 1.23 eV for the photocathode,40 thus lower band gap materials compared to photoanodes are preferred for photocathodes. Fig. 15 shows state-of-the-art examples of photocathodes made from metal oxides, oxy (nitrides), perovskites and PV grade materials. Only a very limited number of metal oxide-based p-type semiconductors are used for photocathodes like Cu2O, CuFeO2, CuBi2O4etc., which show only modest performances.136,137 The most intensively studied metal oxide-based photocathode is Cu2O, which has a band gap of 2.0 eV, and thus can theoretically deliver an ηSTH of 18%. But it is unstable against reduction by photoelectrons and needs proper surface protection for stable operation. Conformal TiO2/AZO protection (buried junction) of Cu2O was first demonstrated by Paracchino et al.138 Electrodeposition of Cu2O on various substrates (Au, Cu, Cu:NiO as a hole transfer layer, HTL) with surface protection resulted in the highest performance for metal oxide-based photoelectrodes of about ∼5 mA cm−2 @0.0 VRHE and stability of ∼2 h.198,199 Luo et al.139 showed that the nanowire morphology of Cu2O greatly increased Jph (10 mA cm−2 at −0.3 VRHE) as compared to planar Cu2O (7.8 mA cm−2) as well as led to marked stability over >55 h by firm protection with RuOx/TiO2/AZO. Very recent demonstration of Pan et al.140 showed an outstanding performance for a metal oxide-based light absorber achieving ∼10 mA cm−2 of Jph as well as Vph near 1.0 V by NiMo/TiO2/Ga2O3/Cu2O as a photocathode. Fig. 15a demonstrates a NiMo-modified, vertically grown Cu2O photocathode that recorded a photocurrent density of 8.2 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE.140
 Fig. 15  Photocathodes for solar hydrogen production. (a) Cu2O nanowire-based NiMo/TiO2/Ga2O3/Cu2O photocathodes in different pH buffer solutions under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 139 and 140 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2016 and Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2018. (b) Pt/TiMo/CdS/Cu2Cd0.4Zn0.6SnS4 and Pt/TiMo/CdS/Cu2ZnSnS4 in 1 M K2HPO4/KH2PO4 solution of pH 3. Reproduced from ref. 141 with permission from Elsevier B. V., copyright 2018. (c) a-SiC, isolated TiO2/a-SiC in 0.5 M potassium hydrogen phthalate electrolyte solution of pH 4. Reproduced from ref. 145 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. (d) Lead halide based perovskite photocathode in 0.1 M borate solution of pH 8.5. Reproduced from ref. 146 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. (e) NiMo/SiO2/Al:np-Si microwire array photocathode. J–V curves for bare SiO2/Al:np-Si, Pt/SiO2/Al:np-Si, and NiMO/SiO2/Al:np-Si microwire arrays in 0.1M H2SO4. Reproduced from ref. 148 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2018. (f) Energy band diagram for the Pt/TiO2/InP photocathode. J–V curves of Pt loaded bare p-InP and TiO2/p-InP photocathodes in 1 M HClO4 solution. Reproduced from ref. 151 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 
The PV-grade light absorbers such as Si, CIGS, III/V semiconductors could be used for photocathodes with a similar working mechanism to a buried junction. However, complete isolation from the electrolyte is mandatory for stable performance of the photocathodes.48 Copper-based chalogenides such as CuInS or Se (CIS), Cu(InGa)Se2 (CIGS), and Cu2ZnSnS4 (CZTS) are promising light absorbers with band gaps around 1.5–1.2 eV, which are tunable by adjusting their elemental composition.16,102 At the moment, this type of light absorber presents the highest performance in Jph (10–35 mA cm−2) as photocathodes as discussed later. Fig. 15b shows the PEC performances of Cu2Cd0.4ZnSnS4 (CCZTS) and CZTS photocathodes coated with Pt/TiMo/CdS in water reduction in a pH 7 electrolyte under 1 sun.141 An enhanced photocurrent was recorded from −4 to −17 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE by introduction of Cd into CZTS. Efficient and robust chalcopyrite based CIGS prototype photocathodes coupled with CdS, an Al2O3 passivation layer and a protective TiO2 layer showed an excellent PEC performance (−30 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE). The prototype photocathode (Pt/TiO2/Al2O3/CdS/CIGS) showed an ABPE of 6.6% in neutral (pH 6.8) electrolyte and 9.3% under acidic (pH 0) conditions with 8 h stability.142 A CdS and Pt surface-modified Cu(In,Ga)Se2 photocathode (Pt/Ti/CdS–Pt/Mo:CIGS) demonstrated efficient solar HER of −27 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE in a neutral electrolyte, and the CIGS photocathode also showed 8.5% of ABPE.143 A photocathode composed of CdS and CdTe treated with CdCl2 [Pt/CdS/CdTe(CdCl2)/Cu] showed a high photocurrent density of −22 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE with 1.1 h stability at pH 8.144 All these PV grade materials make highly efficient photocathodes with an appropriate protection layer to complement their limited stability in aqueous solutions. Yet, their long time durability is still of concern for practical PEC water splitting systems.

Fig. 15c shows an a-SiC-based photocathode with a front surface layer of n-TiO2145 decorated with earth-abundant Ni-Mo as HEC. The Ni-Mo/a-SiC photocathode showed an improved photocurrent density of 2.8 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE as compared to a bare a-SiC photoanode. Introduction of noble metal Pt (Pt/TiO2/a-SiC) showed a photocurrent density of −8.7 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE, while a cheap Ni-Mo catalyzed photocathode, (Ni-Mo/TiO2/a-SiC) showed a similar photocurrent density of −8.3 mA cm−2. The protecting layer of a-TiO2 enhanced the stability up to 12 h at pH 4.
Recently, Micaela et al.146 demonstrated a lead halide perovskite (CH3NH3PbI3)-based photocathode, which showed an interesting PEC performance (Fig. 15d). Thus, the PEDOT:PSS/perovskite/PCBM/PEIE recorded a photocurrent of −9.8 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE in 0.1 M borate (pH 8.5) under 1 sun illumination. Yet, the stability of the material in the electrolyte should be a huge issue (sustained for only ∼1 h) in addition to the complexity of the MAPbI3 solar cell (PSC). However, another demonstration of MAPbI3 particles used as a photocatalyst showed that these kinds of PSCs in electrolytes might be stabilized by unique protection strategies that need to be further developed.147

Fig. 15e shows various Si microwire arrays as a photocathode in 0.1 M H2SO4.148 Without an electrocatalyst, the SiO2/Al:np-Si photocathode showed negligible photocurrents. The Ni-Mo/SiO2/Al:np-Si photocathode showed a photocurrent density of −35.5 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE with stability of 72 h. A photocathode of B and P-doped Si microwire arrays improved the H2 evolution from water. Pt/p-Si and Pt/np-Si of planar morphology showed the highest photocurrent densities of −23 and −28 mA cm−2, respectively, while Pt/p-Si and Pt/np-Si wires showed −7.3 and −15 mA cm−2, respectively. However, only np-Si showed stable and reproducible performance in acidic medium, hence Pt/np-Si performed the best among them.149 Yet another silicon-based photocathode with an epitaxial SrTiO3 protection layer and a nanostructured (Ti–Pt) catalyst (Ti–Pt/SrTiO3/p-Si) showed a photocurrent density of −35 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE, ABPE of 4.9% and 35 h stability in 0.5 M H2SO4.150Fig. 15f shows a band alignment of TiO2/InP photocathodes before and after equilibrium.151 In PEC water reduction in 1 M HClO4 under 1 sun, the Pt/TiO2/InP photocathode showed high onset potential >800 mV and photocurrent of −25.5 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE, which are significantly better than bare Pt/InP. InP nanowires on Si substrates as a photocathode also showed a high photocurrent of −18 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE and 10 h stability in acidic, neutral and basic electrolytes.152

3.4 Unassisted photoelectrochemical water splitting
As discussed above, the performances of photoelectrodes for OER (photoanodes) and HER (photocathodes) are evaluated under external bias. In practice, we need an unbiased, only solar energy-driven overall water splitting (OWS) system, which can be accomplished by using multiple light absorbers including PV. More specifically, unassisted PEC OWS systems are usually realized by constructing tandem cells of photoanode–photocathode, photoanode–PV, or photocathode–PV. These systems are models of practical large scale solar hydrogen production facilities.

3.4.1. Photoanode–photocathode tandem cell. 
For this type of tandem PEC cell, a photoanode (n-type for OER) and a photocathode (p-type for HER) are integrated into a cell through a conductive metal wire or wireless connection with a good ohmic contact. The “wired” configuration is convenient for assembling the components, whereas the “wireless” configuration (also known as “artificial leaf”) is more suitable for a compact design. Two main configurations for the photoanode and photocathode have been developed, one is side-by-side or parallel illumination mode (Mode P), and the other is in series, or tandem illumination mode (Mode T).153 Mode P exposes each photoelectrode to the full solar spectrum without requiring a transparent substrate, whereas Mode T more efficiently utilizes solar energy, with the photoanode positioned in front of the photocathode. Modes T and P illumination-based PEC cells are summarized in Table 3 and Fig. 16. Theoretically a PEC tandem system of photoanode–photocathode configuration can achieve a maximum STH conversion efficiency of 29.7% at a band gap combination of 1.60 eV/0.95 eV.41 In reality, it is very difficult to find such ideal materials of suitable band gaps, band positions, and charge transfer characteristics, and thus measured efficiencies are much lower than the theoretical value.

Reproduced from ref. 154 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2016. (b) α-Fe2O3 photoanode and amorphous Si photocathode in a tandem configuration. J–V plots of α-Fe2O3, rgH II (Fe2O3-II), and NiFeOx-coated rgH II (NiFeOx/Fe2O3-II) photoanodes and the current density of the Si photocathode. The net photocurrent generated during the first 10h of operation using NiFeOx/α-Fe2O3-II and Pt/TiO2/a-Si tandem in 0.5M KPi of pH 11.8 without external bias. Reproduced from ref. 157 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2015. (c) BiVO4-c-Si tandem cell showing the radiation losses on passing through each layer. Reproduced from ref. 148 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2018. (d) An inverse-structure perovskite and PEC tandem cell. The front and back sides of the PEC tandem cell. The J–V curves for a TiCoOx/BiVO4 photoanode (blue), a perovskite-based photocathode (red), and the photocathode in tandem configuration with TiCoOx/BiVO4 light filtering (green). Reproduced with Reproduced from ref. 158 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2018. (e) RuOx/TiO2/Ga2O3/Cu2O photocathode and Mo:BiVO4 photoanode measured in 0.2 M potassium borate of pH 9. Reproduced from ref. 140 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2018. 

Fig. 16a represents a typical photoanode (NiOOH/FeOOH/Mo:BiVO4)–photocathode (Pt/CdS/CuGa3Se5/(Ag,Cu)GaSe2) configuration for overall water splitting under sunlight without external bias.154 The anodic–cathodic J–V curves determine operating potentials of 0.55 VRHE and 0.48 VRHE for parallel and tandem schemes, respectively, at the crossing points of the J–V curves, corresponding to a STH conversion efficiency ηSTH of 0.67%. The PEC tandem cell exhibited a photocurrent of 0.55 mA cm−2 for 2 h without degradation in a neutral phosphate buffer solution. The highest ηSTH for the photoanode–photocathode approach was very recently achieved by a similar type of chalcogenide-based photocathode. Kobayashi et al.155 reported Pt/TiO2/CdS/CIGS/Mo/SLG with ABPE of 12.5% and Jsc of ∼28 mA cm−2 at 0.0 VRHE, which is one of the highest values for photocathodes. With this photocathode and NiFeOx/BiVO4 photoanode, an ηSTH of 3.7% was achieved. Liu et al.156 described a fully integrated PEC tandem system of n-TiO2–p-Si for unassisted water splitting with an ηSTH of 0.12% in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte.156 The lower ηSTH was due to the limited ohmic contact and light-absorption area. Jang et al.157 improved the PEC tandem cell using Mode T configuration of n-type Fe2O3 as a photoanode coated with an NiFeOx overlayer and a-Si as a photocathode coated with a TiO2/Pt overlayer in Ohmic contact.157 The tandem system, NiFeOx/Fe2O3–Pt/TiO2/a-Si, in Fig. 16b achieved an ηSTH of 0.91% and stability of 10 h in pH 11.8 electrolyte. Li and coworkers153 reported a PEC tandem cell using FeOOH/BiVO4 as a photoanode and Pt/Si as a photocathode with Mode P illumination, which showed an ηSTH of 2.5% in a neutral electrolyte.

Fig. 16c introduces a PEC tandem system combining a backside-illuminated photoanode (BiVO4) and a front side-illuminated photocathode (c-Si) in combination with a bipolar membrane.148 The BiVO4–c-Si tandem system showed unbiased water splitting at 0 VRHE for 1 h. Fig. 16d shows an architectural design of a 3D printed versatile PEC tandem cell made of a BiVO4–lead halide perovskite (MAPbI3) solar cell.158 Typical chopped J–V characteristics were measured under 1 sun for a PEC device made of a TiCoOx/BiVO4 photoanode and a Pt/MAPbI3 photocathode and their tandem cell (BiVO4–MAPbI3). The organic–inorganic halide perovskite as a photocathode with NiOx as the HTL showed −12.1 mA cm−2 at 0 V RHE with stability up to 7 h. A PEC tandem cell with a robust TiCoOx/BiVO4 photoanode exhibited 1.31 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE and stability up to 20 h under bias-free operation. The 3D printed PEC tandem cell, BiVO4–MAPbI3 can be scaled up to 10 cm2 for bias-free operation with an ηSTH of 0.35%.158 The 3D printed components of the PEC cell could be easily assembled and adapted for large scale prototypes.
Bornoz et al.159 demonstrated a PEC tandem cell using an n-BiVO4 photoanode and a p-Cu2O photocathode along with Co-Pi and RuOx as OEC and HEC, respectively. The photoelectrodes with an illuminated area of 0.283 cm2 in the PEC tandem system showed an ηSTH of ∼0.87% and stability of >1.5 h at pH 7. Very recently, Pan et al.140 reported a similar PEC tandem cell composed of a NiFeOx/H,Mo:BiVO4 photoanode and a NiMo/TiO2/Ga2O3/Cu2O photocathode as in Fig. 16e. In 0.1 M NaHCO3 (pH 9) under 1 sun illumination, the Cu2O photocathode showed −10 mA cm−2 at 0 VRHE and 100 h stability in a weak alkaline electrolyte. The crossing point of the PEC tandem cell BiVO4–Cu2O system corresponded to −2.4 mA cm−2 and ηSTH of 3% with ∼12 h of stability in 0.2 M KBi (pH 9.0) electrolyte. This is the highest STH from metal-oxide based photoanode–photocathode tandem systems as well as the most stable system yet to be presented.140 Although highly efficient photoanodes and photocathodes are used, the unbiased PEC water splitting system of the photoanode–photocathode tandem cell shows low performance in general due to the mismatch between the two performance curves of the n-type photoanode and p-type photocathode.


3.4.2. Photoelectrode–photovoltaic cells. 
As mentioned, PEC tandem cells of photoanodes and photocathodes depend on well-matched bandgap energies and band edge positions of p-type photocathodes and n-type photoanodes. In contrast, photoelectrode–photovoltaic (PEC–PV) tandem cells are more attractive because there is no such requirement. In the PEC–PV configuration, the band levels of the photoelectrode have no direct relationship with the water redox potentials, because PV cells could supply the required potential, and thus there is less limitation on the material type. However, balancing solar light absorption between the PEC and PV cells should be important for optimization. Thus, a PEC–PV tandem cell with an n-type front light absorber is the most common and favorable geometry, since it can grant high photovoltage to the OER that accounts for the majority of overpotential for water splitting.1
Sivula and coworkers realized various PEC–PV tandem cells, e.g. Fe2O3-DSSC (ηSTH = 1.17%) and WO3-DSSC (ηSTH = 3.1%) for spontaneous overall water splitting without any bias.160 There are several reports on the WO3-DSSC tandem device.161 The results illustrate that developing n-type metal oxide semiconductors and their surface engineering are key factors to achieve better ηSTH. Fig. 17a shows the general scheme of a WO3-DSSC tandem cell, which showed unassisted water splitting with 9 h stability. Hydrogen evolves from the Pt surface attached to the DSSC and oxygen directly from the surface of the WO3 photoanode. The crossing point of J–V curves gives an ηSTH of 3.1%. Fig. 17b shows the unassisted water splitting by using an Al2O3/Si:Fe2O3–MAPbI3 perovskite solar cell tandem. The hematite absorbs high energy photons as a front light absorber and low energy photons are transmitted to the perovskite solar cell. The photo-generated holes are transferred to the OER catalyst to evolve oxygen, while the excited electrons flow to the ohmic contact with the perovskite solar cell. Light absorption in the perovskite cell also generates electron/hole pairs. The holes are driven to the back contact to recombine with the electrons from the hematite photoanode. The excited electrons in the perovskite cell are transferred through a wire to a NiMo hydrogen evolution catalyst to evolve H2 to complete the water splitting. This PEC–PV tandem device (NiFeOx/Al2O3/Si:Fe2O3 and MAPbI3/TiO2 solar cell) demonstrated an ηSTH of 1.9% at 1 VRHE with 8 h stability in pH 13.6 electrolyte.162
 Fig. 17  PEC–PV tandem cells for overall water splitting. (a) WO3-DSSC tandem cell and J–V curves of WO3 (yellow) and DSSC (blue) under simulated 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 160 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2012. (b) A nanostructured α-Fe2O3–perovskite solar cell in a tandem configuration. J–V plots for NiFeOx/Al2O3/α-Fe2O3, Co-Pi/Al2O3/α-Fe2O3, and perovskite (CH3NH3PbI3/TiO2) solar cells after light screening by the top α-Fe2O3 electrode. Reproduced from ref. 162 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. (c) A tandem cell equipped with a hetero-type dual photoanode (HDP) (BiVO4||α-Fe2O3) and a 2p c-Si solar cell. Reproduced from ref. 6 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. (d) A BiVO4/WO3-2jn GaAs/InGaAsP tandem cell where the PV cell operates under reflected light from the photoanode. Reproduced from ref. 163 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2015. (e) A Cu2O–perovskite ((FAPbI)3−x(MAPbI3)x) solar cell–IrO2 tandem cell. J–V curves of the photocathode and anode components with overlaid PV cell. Reproduced from ref. 164 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2015. 
Kim et al.6 reported a novel PEC–PV tandem cell design of hetero-type dual (HDP) photoanodes of NiOOH/FeOOH/H,Mo:BiVO4 and NiFeOx/H,TiO2/Ti:Fe2O3 with a series-connected two parallel (2p) c-Si solar cell with a photovoltage of 1.2 V as the bottom absorber as in Fig. 17c. This system can be classified as a quadruple absorber (‘Q6’) system, i.e. six photons are needed for production of a H2 molecule. The operating point of the HDP type PEC–PV tandem cell showed an ηSTH of 7.7% and 8 h stability, which represents a step forward to efficient design of PEC water splitting. Fig. 17d shows a PEC–PV tandem cell with Co-Pi/BiVO4/WO3 nanorods as the photoanode and a 2jn GaAs/InGaAsP solar cell operating under reflected light from the photoanode.163 The PEC–PV tandem cell showed a stable water splitting photocurrent of 6.56 mA cm−2, which corresponds to an ηSTH of 8.1%. Fig. 17e shows a photocathode–solar cell tandem cell (Cu2O–MAPbI3), where the two light absorbers are placed back-to-back, the PV electron collector is wired to the PEC, and its hole collector is wired to a water oxidation anode. In a dual absorber PEC–PV tandem cell, the Cu2O–MAPbI3 solar cell–IrO2 anode showed unbiased water splitting with an ηSTH of 2.5% and stability of 2.5 h at pH 5.164
The PEC–PV tandem cells need a PV cell that produces bias power using light transmitted through the transparent photoelectrode. Usual PV materials are more expensive than materials for photoelectrodes, hence the cost of the PV component would determine the economic viability of the PEC–PV system. The perovskite solar cells (PSC) attract great attention as a next-generation PV cell because they have a rare combination of high efficiency and low price, although their low stability against moisture delays practical applications. Hence, it is natural to attempt to apply PSC to PEC–PV tandem cells.43
In 2013, Mathews and coworkers165 successfully applied a CH3NH3PbI3 (Eg ∼ 1.55 eV) perovskite PV cell in a PEC–PV tandem cell with a Fe2O3 photoanode for unassisted water splitting to obtain an ηSTH of 2.4% in 1 M NaOH electrolyte, which is twice as high as the 1.17% ηSTH of the Fe2O3-DSSC tandem cell reported by Sivula et al.160 The PEC–PV tandem cell contained a single PSC and a Mn-doped hematite photoanode. However, PSC must be immersed in water electrolyte, which induces an inherent stability problem. As a result, these perovskite-based PEC–PV tandem devices cannot be operated for longer than 1 h. Kim et al.166 fabricated a MAPbI3 perovskite PV cell completely sealed in glass and epoxy for a PEC–PV tandem cell with a BiVO4 photoanode modified with extrinsic (Mo)/intrinsic (H2-treatment) dual doping and a cobalt carbonate (Co-Ci) catalyst. The ηSTH was as high as 4.3% in a “wired” configuration and 3.0% in “wireless” configuration, and the cells were stable for at least 10 h. Groups of Kamat167 and Shen168 also fabricated a MAPbI3 PSC-based PEC–PV tandem cell using Co-Pi loaded BiVO4 or BiVO4/TiO2 as a photoanode, which yielded an ηSTH of 2.5% or 1.24%, respectively. Their activity, however, decreased continuously indicating poor stability. A PEC–PV tandem cell (Co-Pi/W:BiVO4–2jn a-Si) was demonstrated with an ηSTH of 4.9% and 1 h stability at pH 7.3.169 A NiOOH/FeOOH/Mo:BiVO4 – PSC tandem cell showed an ηSTH of 6.2% using single PSC with stability of 5 h.113 Interestingly, Luo et al.170 inverted the PEC-PV configuration from the usual alignment to a PV-PEC configuration using a semitransparent perovskite MAPbBr3 (Eg ∼ 2.3 eV) PV cell in front and a CIGS (Eg ∼ 1.1 eV) as the photocathode. The well matched band gaps enabled efficient panchromatic harvesting of the solar spectrum, resulting in an ηSTH of approximately 6.3%.170
Another approach to PEC–PV tandem is wireless, monolithic devices.156,171 This sheet-like device can function without any external connection and bias, and has a plant's leaf-like structure. This type of devices usually has reduced overall efficiencies compared to wired devices, because of ineffective contacts relative to wiring.172 But such a geometry has lower complexity and may be useful for fabrication of small devices. Fig. 18a and b show nanoscale photoelectrodes (TiO2/a-Si) assembled from inorganic nanowires or nanotree-like heterostructures for direct solar water splitting. The system comprises two semiconductor light absorbers with a large surface area, an interfacial layer for charge transport, and spatially separated cocatalysts to facilitate the water reduction and oxidation. Under simulated sunlight of 150 mW cm−2 (1.5 suns), an ηSTH of 0.12% and 1.5 h stability in 0.5 M H2SO4 were determined.156Fig. 18c shows a tandem junction microwire array with a buried homojunction (n–p+-Si) coated with ITO and WO3.171 The J–V curves in Fig. 18d show the superior performance of tandem junction microwire devices in 1 M H2SO4. Fig. 18e shows the fabrication and structural characterization of the nanotree-like core–shell heterostructures (Co-Pi/W:BiVO4/n–p-Si).173 Fabrication steps include (i) black Si deposition (b) pre-diffusion and etching, and (iii) spray coating of black Si. J–V curves in Fig. 18f show an ηSTH of 0.45% for unassisted solar-driven water splitting for 1 h in 0.5 M KPi electrolyte at pH 7. The small diameter TiO2 nanowires (blue) were grown on the upper half of a Si nanowire (gray), and the two semiconductors absorb different regions of the solar spectrum.
 Fig. 18  Wireless back-to-back connection of PEC–PV tandem. (a) A Si/TiO2 nanotree array with a magnified view of the TiO2 segment used for water oxidation. The photographic images compare a TiO2 nanowire, a Si nanowire, and a Si/TiO2 nanotree substrate. (b) The solar water splitting on nanotree heterostructures under 150 mW cm−2 (1.5 suns) in 0.5 M H2SO4 solution. Reproduced from ref. 156 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2013. (c) A tandem junction microwire array with a buried homojunction (n–p+-Si) coated with ITO and n-WO3, and an energy diagram and reaction pathway for water splitting in the tandem device. (d) J–V curves for single and tandem junction microwire devices in 1.0 M H2SO4. Reproduced from ref. 171 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. (e) Co-Pi/W:BiVO4/SnO2/n-p-Si nanotree heterostructures with black-Si core/W:BiVO4 shell decorated with the CoPi oxygen evolution catalyst. (f) J–V plots of the black-Si core/W:BiVO4 devices for unassisted overall water splitting in 0.5 M KPi. Reproduced from ref. 173 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2017. 
Integrated tandem cells can also be constructed with buried junction-type photoelectrodes. As mentioned above, their extremely high PV efficiency leads to the tandem PEC cell with the highest ηSTH (10–19%), even though it comes with the highest complexity and the highest cost to fabricate. Such difficulty is unfavorable for scale up and practical applications. But again, only this device type accomplished ηSTH above 10%, which is the common target of PEC projects worldwide. The pioneering work of Khaselev and Turner174 on a Pt/GaInP2/GaAs multijunction photoelectrode recorded an ηSTH of 12.4% in 3 M H2SO4 electrolyte under ∼12 concentrated sun condition. The system is not practical because it showed serious corrosion only after 20 h of operation, but the work inspired extensive subsequent research studies on buried junction-type photoelectrodes.

Fig. 19a shows the direct STH conversion via inverted metamorphic multi-junction (IMM) semiconductor architectures as a photocathode, coupled with RuOx as an anode and GaInP/GaInAs as a tandem absorber, which show 60% improvement over the previous high ηSTH of 16% using a tandem III–V device.8Fig. 19b shows a monolithically integrated device, consisting of a GaAs/InGaP photoanode coated with a-TiO2 as a stabilization layer, in conjunction with NiMo as OEC and HEC used for solar-driven water splitting. This unassisted device showed an ηSTH of 10.5% under 1 sun illumination, with stability for 440 h in 1 M KOH. The protected monolithically integrated tandem device is an intrinsically safe prototype system (area ∼ 1 cm2) for a H2 production rate of 0.81 μL s−1 and an ηSTH of 8.6% with separate streams of H2 and O2.175
 Fig. 19  Integrated tandem cell of buried junction type photoelectrodes. (a) An inverted metamorphic multi-junction (IMM) device architecture for maximizing light harvesting with GaInP (Eg = 1.8 eV) and GaInAs (Eg = 1.2 eV) junctions. Reproduced from ref. 8 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2017. (b) Cross-sectional SEM image of a TiO2/InGaP/GaAs photoelectrode (inset: generation of gas bubbles). J–V plots of the Ni/TiO2/InGaP/GaAs photoanode measured in 1.0 M KOH under 1 sun. The short-circuit photocurrent density and the corresponding ηSTH as a function of time in a two-electrode configuration. Reproduced from ref. 175 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. (c) Bifacial GaAs photoelectrode tandem system for unassisted solar water splitting. Separately prepared GaAs photocathode and photoanode are co-integrated on a glass substrate. J–V curves of the GaAs photocathode and photoanode with an ohmic metal contact. Reproduced from ref. 121 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2017. (d) The dual-junction tandem as the photoelectrode for water splitting; Rh/TiO2/AlInPOx/AlInP–GaInP/GaInAs/GaAs/RuOx. The comparison of realized ηSTH and historical development, and theoretical benchmarking values. The PEC performance of the tandem device of the full surface-functionalized photoelectrode and after cap-layer etching. Reproduced from ref. 176 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2018. 

Fig. 19c illustrates a tandem electrode system for unassisted solar water splitting, where separately-prepared GaAs is co-integrated on a glass substrate as both photocathode and photoanode.121J–V curves of printed GaAs photocathode and photoanode with an ohmic metal contact were measured under 1 sun illumination to record an ηSTH of 12.7%. Similarly, Fig. 19d shows a monolithic PEC tandem device of Rh/TiO2/AlInP–GaInP/GaInAs/GaAs/RuO2 for direct water splitting with an ηSTH of 19%.176 Crystalline TiO2 is a corrosion protection layer for the tandem cell, and the monolithic tandem device showed stable performance for 20 h in a neutral electrolyte.


3.4.3. Artificial leaf – monolithic system. 
Monolithic overall water splitting devices are made with compact assembly of light absorbers. This is often called an “artificial leaf” because of its resemblance to the natural photosynthesis of plants, which was termed by Nocera and his coworkers.26 The compact wireless geometry would give less complexity of the device without wires and external connections. As mentioned, the artificial leaf devices developed so far usually underperform relative to corresponding wired devices due to ineffective charge transfers and ohmic contacts. Various combinations of monolithic devices have been developed for photoanode–photocathode, PV–EC, and PEC–PV tandem cells. Most of the demonstrations were made using a buried junction PV protected with an ohmic contact and an electrocatalyst. For monolithic PEC–PV cells, a robust and cheap metal oxide semiconductor like BiVO4 or Fe2O3 can be used as a front light absorber while a solar cell as the back light absorber.6,166
Some examples of artificial leaf device for overall water splitting based on PEC–PV configuration are shown in Fig. 20. In Fig. 20a, Co-Bi/3jn-a-Si/NiMoZn composed of earth-abundant NiMoZn (HEC) and Co-Bi (OEC), and a (3jn) Si solar cell is shown. The device was illuminated for >2 h under AM 1.5 illumination in 1 M KBi (red trace) and 1.5 M KNO3 (blue trace) to observe an ηSTH of 2.5%.26Fig. 20b shows a modulated III–V 3jn solar cell used for a wireless water splitting device (Pt/TiO2/InGaP/GaAs/Ge/IrOx)183 with a total area of 6.25 cm2. The device demonstrated an ηSTH of 11.2% and 7 h of stability in 3 M KHCO3 under 1 sun illumination. Fig. 20c presents a monolithic PEC–PV device composed of a CIGS module and two electrodes deposited with catalysts and encapsulated with epoxy.190 The CIGS-Pt device was illuminated in 3 M H2SO4 under 1 sun to produce a steady gas flow of 1.57 μL s−1 cm−2. Fig. 20d presents an organic artificial leaf (NiMoZn–organic solar cell–Co3O4).100 Using earth abundant catalyst NiMoZn (HEC) and Co3O4 (OEC), ηSTH was 4.9%, while it was 5.4% using RuO2 (OEC and HEC) catalyst for a small area of <0.1 cm2 of the solar cell. Fig. 20e shows an artificial leaf combining in tandem a CH3NH3PbI3 perovskite 1jn solar cell with dual-doped photoanodes Co-Ci/H,Mo:BiVO4 for oxygen evolution.244 The device recorded a PEC performance of 4.8 mA cm−2 in KCi solution (pH 7) under 1 sun illumination and irradiation area 0.56 cm2. It actually produced stoichiometric H2 and O2 with an ηSTH of 3% with an irradiaton area of 1.3 cm2.
 Fig. 20  Monolithic, bias and wire-free “artificial leaf” devices for overall water splitting. (a) A Co-Bi/3jn a-Si/NiMoZn cell under 1 sun in 1 M KBi (red trace) and 1.5 M KNO3 (blue trace). Reproduced from ref. 26 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2011. (b) The generation of H2 and O2 on a modulated III–V triple-junction solar cell (Pt/AgAu/InGaP/GaAs/Ge/IrOx) under 1 sun. Reproduced from ref. 183 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. (c) A monolithic 3 series-connected CIGS solar cell module connected to a Pt electrocatalyst (for both OER, HER), and accumulated gas evolution under 1.0 sun in 3 M H2SO4 electrolyte. Reproduced from ref. 190 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. (d) An organic artificial leaf (RuO2/organic solar cell/RuO2) with RuO2 covered ITO electrodes for H2 (right) and O2 (left) evolution. Reproduced from ref. 100 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. (e) A wireless solar water splitting device without external energy supply by combining in tandem a CH3NH3PbI3 perovskite solar cell with Co-Ci/H,Mo:BiVO4. Reproduced from ref. 166 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 
Artificial leaves based on PV–EC integrate PV and EC components into a single, monolithic device. An efficient device of the multijunction a-Si solar cell connected to CoMo (HEC)–NiFexOy (OEC) was reported by Miller et al.191 with 7.8% STH conversion efficiency and robust durability of over 7200 h in 1 M KOH. Esiner et al.100 demonstrated an organic artificial leaf composed of a 3jn polymer solar cell and NiMoZn (HEC) and Co3O4 (OEC) for H2 and O2 evolution, respectively. This organic leaf showed a STH conversion efficiency of 4.9% with more than 20 min of stability.
A highly versatile artificial leaf based on PV–EC design with floatability and planar compact design is applicable in various natural environments.192 A monolithic device based on CIGS absorbers demonstrated ηSTH above 10% both in PV–EC and PEC–PV configurations.98 A miniature solar water splitting device with 3PV or 4PV solar cell connected to an earth abundant electrocatalyst showed ηSTH values of 7.4% and 6.4%, respectively, using a size of 0.80 cm.2,157 A CuInS2 solar cell and Pt (HEC) and NiFeOx/Ni (OEC) catalysts of a planar design module for large-scale artificial leaves showed an ηSTH of 3.14% and H2 gas evolution of 83 μmol in 150 min.

3.5 Summary of laboratory results of solar water splitting
In this section, we graphically summarize in Fig. 21 the performance of various solar water splitting systems discussed so far in terms of STH conversion efficiency ηSTH and stability for three system categories of PV–EC, PEC, and PC based on data summarized in Tables 1–3. Similar summaries were presented in 2014 by Ronge et al.15,21 and in 2015 by Ager et al.46 Here we update the evolution of ηSTH, and add the trend of stability and the light absorber's size, which are also important factors for practical solar hydrogen production. In addition, we subdivide PEC devices into PE buried (devices similar to PV cells but function in water with sophisticated surface protection), PE–PV (at least one side of the device directly contacts water forming a semiconductor||liquid junction, SLJ), and PE–PE (made with only photoelectrodes) devices, all having a main light absorber working inside of the electrolyte. In contrast, PV–EC devices work outside of the electrolyte, and PC devices are free or fixed powders with no charge collector or photoelectrodes.
 Fig. 21  A comparison of photon-driven water splitting systems. (a) Reported STH from various device types (PV–EC, PEC and PC). PEC devices are further classified: PE buried indicates devices similar to PV cells but functioning in water by surface protection. PE–PV indicates that at least one side of the device directly contacts water forming a semiconductor||liquid junction (SLJ). PE–PE is a device made with only photoelectrodes. (b) Stability and (c) the device's light absorber's geometric size with reported STH. (d) Golden triangles for a practical solar hydrogen production system and their simplified evaluation. Left side is for PV–EC, PEC and PC and right side is for PEC related devices. 
In general, PV–EC systems clearly show the highest ηSTH (up to 20% by III/V, ∼10% by Si), followed by PEC (3.7% for PE–PE, ∼8.1% for PE–PV, ∼19% for PE buried), and then PC (mostly around 1.0%) systems. Also there is an appreciable trend of steady improvement in ηSTH over the last two decades. The PEC systems show the largest variation of ηSTH because of the largest system variation. Thus, the systems of PE buried show the most complexity and the highest ηSTH, while PE–PE systems of the minimum complexity show the lowest ηSTH. Unfortunately, none of those experimentally studied systems meet the criteria for practical solar hydrogen production, i.e., a high efficiency above 10%, a long lifetime over 10 years, and scalability up to several km2, which we discuss in more detail in later parts of this paper.
Benchmarking stability is not simple because the reported period of stability does not necessarily mean the lifetime of the device. But the trend is clearly seen. The best stability is observed with PC or PEC systems using intrinsically stable oxide semiconductors or C3N4. However, there are few p-type semiconductors that are stable under photocathode conditions. Many successful cases of protection layer strategies have been reported for unstable p-type or PV grade materials. However, the practical devices require durability as long as 10 years, and longevity of the protection layers for such a time scale is of concern. There is a review specifically addressing the stability issue of photoanodes.48 It also should be mentioned at this point that efficiency and durability are only two of the many factors that determine the economic competitiveness of the system as will be discussed in later sections.
4. Scaling-up of solar water splitting system: artificial leaf to tree and to farm
Due to the rapid progress of past decades in the basic research of efficient and durable solar water splitting materials and systems, it is about time to consider practical applications through scale-up.193 Wind or PV-powered PEM electrolyzers have been well investigated to semi-commercial levels,72 but PEC cells and PC systems have not been explored in such a depth of development. Hence, development of cost-effective, functional, scalable, deployable and practical solar hydrogen production system is in dire need considering the ever-increasing demand for a climate-friendly energy carrier. The PEC water splitting has the potential of meeting the above requirements, yet numerous challenges are involved such as: (i) improved efficiency of photoelectrode materials; (ii) reduced internal resistance losses; (iii) long lifetime, especially of the photoelectrodes; (iv) maximal photon absorption; (v) efficient gas separation and storage; (vi) scaling-up and (vii) reduced plant capital costs.194 All the requirements are needed for reduction of H2 production cost to be competitive with those of the current commercial processes of steam reforming of natural gas (∼1 $ per kg) or electrolysis of water (∼5.5 $ per kg14). DOE's target for the cost of solar H2 production is 4 $ per kg by 2020 for commercialization.15 In this section, we discuss the feasibility of scale up of the laboratory results addressing techno-economic aspects of the solar water splitting technologies and introducing large-scale prototypes and demonstration projects.
4.1 Factors to consider in scale up
First consideration in the scale up is that the overall efficiency of the scaled up device is inevitably lower than the small scale laboratory devices regardless of system type due to unique factors involved in the process as qualitatively discussed in Fig. 22. In Fig. 22a and b, a photoanode and photocathode pair with band gaps of 1.84 eV and 1.23 eV and their tandem cell (D4) are shown, which would achieve an ηSTH of 22% theoretically according to calculation by Pinaud et al.40 The assumed “ideal device” has a total photovoltage of 1.68 V and thus shows an onset potential of −0.45 V in a two electrode mode. Faradaic efficiency of gas evolution was assumed to be 100%. The performance of a real laboratory scale device (scale of 1–10 mm and an assumed size of 0.1 cm2) is lowered by overpotentials of OER (∼0.3 V) and HER (∼0.1 V),77 charge recombination in the bulk/surface of the semiconductor, surface Fermi level pinning (which will be dominant if the light absorber forms an adaptive junction), decreased light harvesting by reflection, and so on. Thus experimentally achievable STH efficiency is reduced to 15% with reduced photovoltage by ∼0.15 V and current loss by 35% (Fig. 22c). It should be noted that the total current load for the device is only ∼1.2 mA, which would not induce any significant potential gradient by reactants (H+), and the distance between electrodes is rather small, thus a laboratory scale device is often free from a large ohmic loss.
 Fig. 22  Efficiency loss in scaled-up photoanode–photocathode tandem cells. (a) Device model on the experimental scale. (b) Band gap alignment. (c) J–V curves of an ideal device with no energetic loss and a real experimental device with known loss mechanisms. (d) Scaled-up tandem photoelectrochemical device based on cell architecture.29,30 (e) J–V curves of the experimental device and the scaled up device with expected energy losses. 
When the device is scaled up to a scale of ∼1 m or much larger (10 m2 panel, 106 times larger than laboratory scale), there are problems that are not usually considered at small scales (Fig. 22d). First, making a bigger device requires different fabrication methods, and usually accompanies spatial defects195 and increased total Ohmic resistance,196 which would decrease the efficiency lower than 15%. Another thing that large solar hydrogen production systems need is fuel separation, which is usually done by a membrane separator. Only a few studies are available28–30,41,172,197 on membranes applied for PEC cells, not like the extensive studies in the fields of fuel cells and PEM electrolyzers. Studies showed surprisingly high energy loss from simply applying a good membrane and large potential and current losses are expected from the fuel separator. Also, the light intensity is usually less than the standard 100 mW cm−2 in many places of the world and not uniform over a large area, which will further lower the overall performance of the device.195,196
Finally, the above factors would affect to different degrees depending on cell architectures and materials (light absorbers, electrocatalysts, substrates, housing, electrolytes, etc.) of a scaled up PEC system. Thus from a theoretical (ideal) device to experimental (laboratory), and then to large-size scaled-up devices, continuous efficiency loss is inevitable (Fig. 22e), and it is one of the major tasks of scale up to minimize the losses. Here we assume modest loss in current (33%) and potential (∼0.15 V), which results in STH efficiency of ∼10% for the panel size device, which is the commonly noted efficiency for practical solar hydrogen production by a PV–EC device.
4.2 Techno-economic assessment
There are attempts to assess the state of the solar hydrogen production in comparison with existing technologies of hydrogen production. Ardo et al.198 recently proposed readiness perspective about solar hydrogen production in the time scale of 2050 in comparison with already existing ones. They also tried to identify pathways to implementing solar-hydrogen technologies. Particularly, the fields of market opportunities for short term (10 years of service time) are also noted such as grid-level energy storage, transportation, local or isolated permanent energy systems, military industry, agriculture sector, high-margin products, and for long term like space industry. For specific examples of solar to hydrogen production systems, PV–EC (e.g. Jia et al., ηSTH of 30%88) and PEC (e.g. Verlage et al., ηSTH of 10%175) were compared for a similar light absorber class of III–V semiconductors, which is quite complex but highly efficient. It was shown that the PV–EC system clearly outperformed the PEC cell at the technology readiness level.198
Recently, the Royal Society conducted a technology readiness level (TRL) investigation to show the current state of solar fuel production technologies in comparison with conventional technologies.199 Steam methane reforming and the alkaline electrolyser were rated as TRL 8 and ‘mature’, and solar fuel production was rated at a low TRL of 1–3, meaning it is still in the stage of basic technology research. It is even lower than PEM electrolysis (TRL: 5–7). Yet, they predicted that solar fuel production, especially by PEC or PC, would present lower capital costs. In addition, CO2 tax is also going to be a major issue, for which solar-to-fuel technology is an option that is fully free from CO2. Nuclear energy is also carbon-free,200 but it is out of the scope of this review (Fig. 23).
 Fig. 23  Technical readiness level (TRL) of solar hydrogen production technology. (a) Scheme representing PV–electrolysis and PEC device concepts, including current relative level of use, projected cost, required amount of raw materials, and current relative level of technology readiness. Reproduced from ref. 198 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. (b) Technology readiness level evaluation alongside of solar to fuel technology to other ones. Reproduced from ref. 199 with permission from the Royal Society, copyright 2018. 
In this early stage of development, techno-economic assessment for practical solar hydrogen production systems is mandatory, starting from why such a scale-up is needed in spite of already existing technologies of hydrogen production. Indeed, there are a number of published perspective and techno-economic assessment papers including Ronge et al.,15,21 Zhai,28 Sathre et al.,29,30 Pinaud et al.40 and Shaner et al.,14 and an overview of such studies.172
An economic analysis of solar H2 production by the U.S. Department of Energy estimated that the PC systems with an ηSTH of 5% can produce H2 at a cost of $2.30 kg−1. The STH efficiency data summarized in Table 1 reveal that we are not far from realization of 5% STH efficiency by PC solar H2 production. The results suggest that the PC approach could be a promising strategy for solar H2 production because of its extremely simple reactor design and ease of scale up. However, it requires separation of the explosive H2 and O2 gas mixture.

Fig. 24 exhibits a few selected examples of scaled-up photocatalytic H2 production systems. In 2015 Schröder et al.63 reported the use of Pt-loaded mesoporous carbon nitride (Pt/mp-C3N4) to design a large scale PC solar H2 production reactor of nearly 1 m2 area under natural sun light (Fig. 24a). This reported reactor had an ηSTH of 0.12% and produced a total 18.2 L per month of H2. In another large scale PC solar H2 production system, Goto et al.64 emphasized the use of an easily expandable panel system (Fig. 24b). The 1 m2 panel actually consisted of 3 × 3 RhCrOx + CoOx/Al:SrTiO3 photocatalytic sheets of 33 × 33 cm2 area. This reported scale up system had a better ηSTH (0.4%) than the earlier one. They also emphasized the importance of continuous release of gas bubbles from the photocatalytic surface and proposed a 1 mm deep water layer for the purpose.64 To resolve the gas separation issue in the PC system, Osterloh and Parkinson201,202 reported the utilization of a hydrogen selective polymeric membrane as schematically illustrated in Fig. 24c.201,202 The selective diffusion of photogenerated H2 through the polymeric membrane led to H2 separation from the mixed gas. The Z-scheme solar water splitting in a vertically stacked particle-suspension photocatalytic reactor in Fig. 24d was proposed by Chandran et al.203
 Fig. 24  A few selected examples of scaled-up PC H2 production systems: (a) Photo-reactor composed of a Pt/C3N4 PC sheet of ∼1 m2. The averaged H2 evolution reaction rates from the reactor with respect to the irradiated energy and those of the laboratory-scale experiment (horizontal dashed line). Reproduced from ref. 63 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2015. (b) A 1 m2 Al:SrTiO3 panel for long term overall water splitting. The detailed assembly of this 1 m2 Al:SrTiO3 water splitting panel of 9 photocatalyst squares (each 33 × 33 cm2). The H2 evolution performance of a 5 cm2 RhCrOx + CoOx/Al:SrTiO3 panel. Reproduced from ref. 64 with permission from Elsevier B.V., copyright 2018. (c) A circulating photocatalytic water splitting reactor consisting of hydrogen selective polymeric membrane pipes for selective separation of H2. Reproduced from ref. 201 with permission from the Cambridge Press, copyright 2011. (d) The Z-scheme solar water splitting in a vertically stacked particle-suspension photocatalytic reactor. Reproduced from ref. 203 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. 
For large scale solar hydrogen production, the PV–EC system is steps ahead of PEC or PC cells. Rodriguez and Moser204 analyzed a PV–EC system with Si PV (11% 3jn a-Si/a-Si/uc-Si) as a model light absorber and varying other balance of system (BOS) like electrocatalyst (OER/HER), housing, and membrane for calculating hydrogen price (Fig. 25). Results showed an excellent hydrogen cost below $2.90 kg−1 even with PV of the modest efficiency (Fig. 25b). In addition, the calculation shows that nearly 97% of cost comes from the PV component and that electrocatalysts contribute insignificantly even with expensive IrOx/Pt for the PV–EC system. The use of IrOx/Pt gave the highest ηSTH of ∼8.26% while cheaper RuO2/NiMo catalysts gave ∼8.17%, and thus the latter provided a better price option.
 Fig. 25  (a) Cost analysis for integrated PV–EC systems with a 3jn a-Si/a-Si/uc-Si solar cell. (b) Cost comparison of solar-hydrogen generators that incorporate different catalysts. The results show that the PV component dominates the price of H2 produced in optimized systems. F and average STH efficiencies (η) are shown for each system. Reproduced from ref. 204 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. 
Sathre et al.29 conducted a life-cycle net energy assessment (LCA) for a 1 GW (continuous annual average) PEC hydrogen production facility (1 GW HHV = 610 metric tons of H2 per day)29 based on a basic capacity calculation previously conducted by Zhai et al.28 Their calculation started from assuming a model system and the calculation was conducted by Oracle® Crystal Ball software. In Fig. 26, four hierarchical levels of a solar hydrogen production facility are depicted – (1) a PEC “cell” of 2 m2; (2) a truck-transportable panel of 29 m2 comprising multiple cells; (3) a “field” of 1000 panels plus gas compression and storage, occupying 12 ha; and (4) the 1 GW (continuous annual average) “facility” of 1510 fields occupying 180 km2 of land. The facility was assumed to be located at southwest US.
 Fig. 26  Four hierarchical levels of a 1 GW (continuous annual average) hydrogen production facility. (a) The full size of the PEC cell, shown partially in cut-away view, is 1.7 m × 1.2 m. (b) PEC panel with dimensions of 12.0 m × 2.4 m. Flow rates are based on base-case conditions, and do not include water vapor lost with vented O2. (c) Diagram of a PEC field, comprising 1000 panels (100 rows of ten panels) plus compression and storage equipment for one day's production of H2 (and O2, if collected). (d) Diagram of a PEC facility producing 1 GW (annual average) H2. Reproduced from ref. 29 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. 
In system assessment studies for practical scales, one needs to consider balance of system (BOS) in addition to the main components of light absorber and reactor, which includes the supporting structure, manifolds/pipes, pumps/compressors, storage tanks for H2, roads and monitor/controller. As shown in Fig. 27a, Sathre et al.31 conducted assessment of the initial energy input for 1 GW H2 production facility, which includes 3 big parts of light active cell, inactive module (reactor) and the BOS of the system. BOS is responsible for almost ∼50% of energy input for the system. In order to show the actual BOS components, Fig. 27b introduces a large scale PEC–PV system composed of a BiVO4 photoanode and Si PV with size of 1.284 m × 1.613 m and with a maximum H2 production rate of 7.7 L h−1 (=∼0.68 g h−1) constructed by a consortium between UNIST and Elchemtech Co., Ltd of South Korea. For the PEC cell, BiVO4 was fabricated on FTO using Sayama's auto combustion method205 using water-based solvent. Thus, ∼20 cm2 sized NiFeOx/BiVO4/FTO achieved ∼1.5 mA cm−2 at 1.23 V vs. counter electrode, which is similar to the performance of PECDEMO's BiVO4 (∼50 cm2). The overall performance of the PEC–PV tandem cell is also similar to that of the Artiphyction prototype (maximum ηSTH ∼2%). Notably, they achieved complete H2 separation from mixed gas products to feed to a fuel cell. Fig. 27b shows BOS of the system–flowmeter, control panel, pipes, steel frame, H2 separator, and compressor.
 Fig. 27  Composition assessment of balance of system in a photoelectrochemical cell. (a) One-time primary energy inputs (PJ) required to construct and decommission a 1 GW (annual average) facility. For comparison, the base-case annual delivered net energy of the completed facility in operation is shown as a blue dashed line. Reproduced from ref. 29 and 30 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014 and 2016. (b) Proof in concept scaled-up PEC–PV device. Left-side picture image of 1 m2 NiFeOx/BiVO4/FTO with 0.2 m2 c-Si panel (left) and control panel connected to hydrogen separation modules. Reproduced with courtesy of Elchemtech and UNIST consortium. 
Based on mass and energy balance, the life-cycle primary energy balance describes the amount of energy provided by the facility to society during its life span. In units of PJ, it is calculated as the total energy output minus the total energy input, and it is given by the following equation: Energy balance = {T × EH} − {Ep + (T × EO)} + ED(7)
The energy return on energy invested (EROEI or EROI) describes how much usable energy will be delivered by the facility or system, relative to its required energy inputs. EROEI is unitless, and it is calculated as the total energy output divided by the total energy input: (8)
In (4) and (5), T = service life of the facility (years); EH = energy (HHV) in hydrogen produced in 1 year (PJ per year); EP = energy used to produce the facility (PJ); EO = energy used to operate the facility for 1 year (PJ per year) and ED = energy used to decommission the facility (PJ). For actual operation, energy balance must be greater than 0 and EROEI must be greater than 1.
The energy payback time describes how long the facility must operate for it to deliver H2 equivalent of the energy required for its manufacturing, construction, and decommissioning. Energy payback time (EPBT) of practical PEC technology depends on materials and operation: (9)
EPBT is not a life-cycle metric, i.e. energy is not delivered after the payback time; hence above eqn (6) does not include the variable T, the facility service life.
Multiple factors are considered including ηSTH (5, 10, 20%) and life span (5, 10, 20 and 40 years) of the PEC panel, fabrication of photoactive materials, other materials and capacity factor, solar transmittance loss, and gas storage capacity to determine energy balance, EROEI, EPBT, while the base case was set for ηSTH of 10% and a lifetime of 10 years. Analysis showed EBPT of 8.1 years and EROEI of 1.6. Analysis showed very large dependence of capital cost of panel replacement, which was the cost of the photoelectrode (Fig. 28b) and return of benefits also greatly depended on the efficiency and life span of this panel (Fig. 28c). For instance, an increase in ηSTH from 5% to 20% shortened the EBPT from 17.9 years to only 3 years with a life span of 20 years, and elongation of lifetime from 5 to 20 years shortened the EBPT from 18.2 years to 6.4 years for an ηSTH of 10%. The results indicate that the base case performance (ηSTH of 10% and a lifetime of 10 years) is required for making the scaled up device in any sense.
 Fig. 28  Life-cycle net energy assessment based on a model facility for 1 GW HHV. (a) Annual energy balance of 1 GW H2 production facility in operation (PJ per year). Energy use for panel replacement is based on steady-state conditions with 10 year life span (10% of panels replaced each year) and base-case energy use for cell materials and fabrication. Error bars for “panel replacement” show 5- and 20 year life spans. Error bars for “panel heating” are based on the coldest and warmest geographic locations. Error bars for other categories are based on Monte Carlo simulation. (b) Change in base-case energy payback time (EPBT) due to variation of individual parameters between low and high estimates. (c) Life cycle net energy metrics for a 1 GW H2 production facility, under various assumptions of facility life span, specific cell life span (20 years) with variation of ηSTH (5, 10, and 20%). The numbers in bold represent base-case conditions. Reproduced from ref. 29 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. 
Recently, Shaner et al.14 compared solar energy-driven H2 production cost by PEC cells (type 3, and 4 configuration of Pinaud et al.40), PV–EC, and with grid-supplemented EC (GSPV–EC) as in Fig. 29. Calculation was made assuming H2 production of 3.65 kiloton per year and price for PV-electricity of 0.03 $ per kW h,206 which is a critical factor for comparison. With base case (ηSTH of 9.76% for systems, PV efficiency of 16% and nearly 60% electrolyzer efficiency), the levelized cost of hydrogen (LCH) and capital cost of Type 3 systems are $11.4 kg−1 and $205MM dollars and areal capital cost is $293 m−2 with major price factor coming from the semiconductor ($48) and membrane ($50), which are lower than LCH for the comparable base-case PV–EC system with LCH of $12.1 kg−1 and $260MM ($371 m−2). The type 4 system had an even lower LCH of $9.2 kg−1 and $160MM ($428 m−2), while semiconductors (InGaP/GaAs) ($175) and light concentrator ($48) took the 1st and 2nd highest price portions. The lowest LCH was expected from GSPV–EC with LCH of 5.5 $ per kg and $34 MM for water electrolysis, while the control case-steam methane reforming (SMR) showed LCH of only 1 $ per kg, which is currently the cheapest way to produce hydrogen.
 Fig. 29  (a) Block diagram depicting the power flow through a PEC plant (left) and PV–EC system. The insets show Type 3 and 4 systems studied by Pinaud et al.40 Right side is for the PV–EC system with or without grid assistance.
