(b) Contour plots of the LCH ($ per kg H2) for the aforementioned systems as a function of the plant efficiency and active component (PEC cell for type 3 and 4, PV modules and electrolyzers for PV–EC) areal capital expense normalized by the required solar collection area ($ per m2) for PEC systems and electrolyzer capital for PV–EC ($ per W generated). The base-case result is indicated by the yellow circle. Contours are labeled at $2 per kg intervals. (c) Summary plot. Reproduced from ref. 14 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2016. 
Obviously, all systems would have lower LCH with higher system efficiencies and lower capital costs of the system, as well elucidated in Fig. 29b. With the perspective of improved efficiency (25% is assumed to be the practical limit) and lowering capital cost (100–200$ for base case), LCH of 3–4 $ per kg is expected, which is still higher than SMR. However, global energy scenario and CO2 emission should be considered in actual selection of technology to implement.198 In addition, we need to find a way to circumvent such “seeming limits” of solar hydrogen production technologies. In conclusion, significantly higher energy-return on energy-invested (EROI) and lower LCH should be achieved to realize practical solar hydrogen production.
4.3 Selection of materials
For practical large scale systems, the criteria of materials selection should be different from those of laboratory devices because the cost competitiveness should be a primary concern. Complexity of the device is considered as a major factor for differentiating PV–EC and PEC, which often employ similar materials.190,207 Jacobsson et al.190,207 made a simple criterion whether the photoelectrode or the light absorber works inside or outside of the electrolyte. Recently Yao and Li102 proposed that the materials should be classified as PV grade (c-Si, a-Si, GaAs, GaP, InP, GaInP, CdS, CdSe, CdTe, ZnSe, ZnTe, CuInS2, CuInGaSe2etc.), and non-PV grade (TiO2, WO3, Fe2O3, BiVO4, GaN:ZnO, TaON, Ta3N5, BaTaO2N, Cu2O, CuWO4, CaFe2O4), since PV grade material-based photoelectrodes in fact often share or even have exactly the same structure and geometry as those of PV materials. Ronge et al.15,21 also cited lower complexity as a potential merit of the PEC system over PV–EC, which would directly affect the capital cost of the scaled up system.
The material's complexity could be represented by “layers”, and reducing the number of layers would be a direction of photoelectrode development for scale up with capital cost saving. As schematically shown in Fig. 30, highly active photoelectrodes (photocurrent above ∼10 mA cm−2 at short circuit voltage) always involve more than 2 layers (protection + electrocatalyst) and often 3 layers (charge collector + protection layer + electrocatalyst). This is owing to the fact that light absorbers with good charge mobility and light harvesting capability are PV grade non-oxide materials, which are generally expensive as well as unstable in water and thus need protection.48,208 To reduce the complexity of photoelectrodes, light absorber itself is better to be air- and water-stable requiring less degree of protection.16 Hence, a material's capital cost expected from its complexity should be balanced out for its efficiency. Of course, an efficient, stable, and cheap material would be ideal for scale up, which is not yet available, and in reality, we need to make a smart compromise.
 Fig. 30  (a) Reported photocurrent density under short circuit conditions (Jsc) of photoelectrodes for PEC water splitting under standard conditions: 1.0 sun, short circuit condition of 1.23 VRHE for photoanode, and 0.0 VRHE for photocathode. Materials and references: TiO2,106 WO3,209 BiVO4,108,210,211 TaON,212 Cu2O,140 Fe2TiO5,213 Fe2O3,6 Ta3N5,128 BaTaO2N,127 BaNbO2N,123 CuFeO2,214,215 CuBi2O4,216 p-MAPbI3,146,217 n-CdTe,134 p-CdTe,133 ACGSe,218 CuInS2,219 CZTS,141 CIGS,220 p-Sb2Se3,221 p-InP,151 n-InGaN,126 n-GaAs,131 p-GaP,222 n-GaP,131 SiC,145 n-Si,135 p-Si.148 (b) Known composition of photoelectrodes in (a). 
To achieve ηSTH above 10% for instance, Jph of ∼8.13 mA cm−2 should be generated by the water splitting reaction, so band gap should be smaller than ∼2.3 eV. But as shown in Fig. 30, it is rare to find materials other than PV grade that achieved Jph above 8.0 mA cm−2. The value is above the theoretical limit of BiVO4, the best metal oxide-based photoanode at the moment. As the photocathode, Cu2O achieved the highest Jph (10 mA cm−2 at 0.0 VRHE) as well as a photovoltage of ∼1.0 V, which are remarkable for a metal oxide light absorber. Semi-stable Ta3N5 recorded Jph ∼12 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE. Besides them, most of the photoelectrodes showing Jph above 8.0 mA cm−2 are based on PV grade light absorbers with buried junctions and protection layers, which make their complexity rather high. Notably, p- or n-Si, III/V based semiconductors, CIS and CIGS achieved Jph at short circuit above 15–20 mA cm−2. But their photovoltages are rather low, around 0.6–1.0 V. In conclusion, photoelectrodes showing high ηSTH above 10% are limited mainly to PV grade light absorbers of limited stability.
As mentioned, most of the high performance PEC water splitting systems utilize PV-grade light absorbers and noble metal-based electrocatalysts. Even if a high efficiency system is successfully developed, it may not make a practical system if it is made of rare and precious materials. Hence, elemental abundance is certainly an important aspect to consider for scale-up not only for solar H2 generation but many other industries.223

Fig. 31a presents a relation of abundance in the Earth's crust, annual production, and cost (pure form) of some common elements.224–227 It demonstrates that the global production of the materials (from U.S. Geological Survey 2018) is not necessarily consistent with their crustal abundance, but instead reflects their demand. Since renewable H2 production will be a huge market in the future, the current research should focus more on earth abundant materials that can substitute the high valued materials in functionality.223,228–231Fig. 31b summarizes the three different aspects of materials; earth crust abundance to highlight the availability, yearly production to demonstrate the demand, and cost of pure element to signify the complexity of the processing process of the essential elements used in PEC water splitting systems. Moreover, a simplistic view has been presented of the sustainable clean energy production depending on available reserves of elements and earth abundance of photoactive materials.
 Fig. 31  (a) Annual production, abundance in the Earth's crust, and cost (pure form) of common elements used in PEC water splitting.224,226,227,232 (b) Relation between available reserves of elements, earth abundance, and supply risk of photoactive materials for sustainable clean energy production.233 
4.4 Fabrication techniques on a large scale
In addition to the choice of materials, the fabrication method should also be suitable for scale-up.175 For scaling-up the PEC water splitting systems, we have to consider first whether we can apply the laboratory fabrication techniques on a large scale such as metal organic chemical vapor deposition (MOCVD),121,234 molecular beam epitaxy (MBE),60,126,235 atmospheric pressure chemical vapor deposition (APCVD), atomic layer deposition (ALD), pulsed laser deposition (PLD), sputtering, sol–gel or solution based coating (MOD, PADD) via spray pyrolysis, screen printing and spin coating, etc. The technique giving the highest efficiency is not always the choice in large scale fabrication. In general, solution-based methods near ambient conditions are desirable without harsh pressure, temperature, vacuum, and inert gas environment.31,104 Fabrication of PV grade materials (GaP, GaAs, InP, GaInP2, c-Si, a-Si, CIS, CIGS etc.) in a large size requires different strategies as well established in PV applications. However, it should be noted that the III/V material classes made by MOCVD236 and similar methods requiring O2-free atmosphere contributed significantly to the development of high efficiency PEC and PV–EC systems (Tables 2, 3 and Fig. 21).
Many metal oxide semiconductors are inherently stable in air, and nearly the whole synthetic process could be conducted under ambient conditions. The hydrothermal method is a simple and widely used technique for making both particle and film types. As shown in Fig. 32a, a WO3 nanoparticle array (NPA) film with sizes above 400 cm2 was made by the hydrothermal method.237 Hydrothermal synthesis of FeOOH from Fe salt at 80–120 °C and its conversion into Fe2O3 film are widely used for fabricating Fe2O3 nanorod films (Fig. 32d).32,119,120Fig. 32e shows electrodeposition of Cu2O films with a sequential deposition process on ∼50 cm2 FTO for the PECDEMO project.238,255
 Fig. 32  Solution-based methods for photoelectrodes on a modest scale (>5 cm2). (a) Hydrothermal synthesis of WO3 NPA films on substrates of various sizes. Reproduced from ref. 237 with permission from Elsevier B.V., copyright 2017. (b) Metal organic decomposition deposition of BiVO4 on FTO films with 5 × 5 cm2 scale. Reproduced from ref. 250 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2016. (c) Electrodeposition of BiOI (red) and thermal conversion to BiVO4 films (yellow). Reproduced with permission of ref. 109 and 253 with permission from the American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2014 and Elsevier B.V., copyright 2015. (d) Hydrothermal synthesis of Fe2O3. (d) Spray pyrolysis deposition of Fe2O3 in a large area (>100 cm2). Reproduced from ref. 254 copyright 2015, imperial college UK (DOI: https://www.imperial.ac.uk/media/imperial-college/research-centres-and-groups/electrochemical-engineering/5–10thESEE_Photo-electrochemical-Production-of-H2.pdf). (e) Electrodeposition of Cu2O films with a sequential deposition process, reproduced from ref. 238 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2017. The right side image is for the Cu2O film deposited on ∼50 cm2 FTO. Reproduced from ref. 255. PECDEMO is funded by the European Fuel Cell and Hydrogen Joint Undertaking (FCH-JU) (DOI: http://www.fch.europa.eu/project/photoelectrochemical-demonstrator-device-solar-hydrogen-generation). 
Metal organic decomposition (MOD) is also a versatile and scalable method invented by Galembeck et al,239 and applied first to the synthesis of BiVO4 by Sayama et al.240,241 It requires a metal precursor decomposable (preferably organometals like acetate, acetyl acetate, oleate) in organic solvents (acetyl acetonate, acetic acid, or methanol).104,239,242 Since MOD denotes the nature of the precursor and solvent, the deposition can be varied from drop casting,243 spin coating,244 screen printing,245 spray pyrolysis,169,189,246–248etc. Yao et al.249 reported BiVO4/WO3/FTO, in which MOD BiVO4 was drop cast on substrates as large as 25 cm2. They also found that efficiency dropped by 50% when the film size increased from 0.125 cm2 to 2.0 cm2. Kim and Lee250 showed microwave-assisted synthesis of BiVO4 using a MOD precursor, within immensely short 5–8 minutes of synthesis time compared to usual 60–300 min for traditional thermal synthesis (Fig. 32b). The MOD is a branch of the sol–gel method which has been widely applied on various scales. For photoelectrode fabrication, Hong et al.111,251 showed polymer assisted decomposition deposition (PADD) for a WO3 photoanode and Kim et al.6 reported a Fe2O3 film made with similar PADD with PEG polymer.
Electrodeposition is very useful for making light absorber materials.31 In fact, multi-component photoelectrodes processed in solution are often made by electrodeposition like NiFeOx or Co-Pi OEC for Fe2O3162 and BiVO4,252 NiMo HEC for Cu2O photocathodes.140 It is one of the most useful methods available for non-PV-grade photoelectrode fabrication as recently reviewed by Kang and Choi.31 Many materials including SnO2, TiO2, ZnO, In2O3, WO3, Fe2O3, BiVO4, CuWO4, Cu2O, CuFeO2, CuBi2O4, CIS, CIGS, CZSS etc. could be synthesized by the electrodeposition method under mild conditions, yet giving high efficiencies. Electrocatalysts such as MOOH (M = Co, Fe, Ni, Mn, etc.), IrO2, RuO2, various metal alloys and Pt could also be deposited by electrodeposition in large scale fabrication. Specifically, Choi and coworkers109,253 developed highly porous BiVO4 on FTO by electrodeposition and thermal conversion of BiOI that showed extremely high efficiency for PEC water oxidation (Fig. 32c). There is a subsequent report on the synthesis of BiVO4 photoanodes of higher efficiency, but it involves glancing angle deposition (GLAD) of WO3163 and nanostructuring of SiO2 by sputter/E-beam deposition,113 thus it is not suitable for large scale fabrication, although scaling up of the method was also attempted by fabricating a ∼25 cm2-sized film.253
4.5 Charge collectors or substrates
Another basic component of the PEC cell is the charge collector or substrate such as transparent conductive oxides (TCO) like F:SnO2 (FTO), In:SnO2 (ITO), and Al:ZnO (AZO), as well as metals like Ti, Au, and Mo. The TCOs are preferred substrates in order to construct a tandem system of multiple light absorbers for unassisted water splitting. The importance of the selection of the substrate for photoelectrodes has been greatly underrated especially when PV-grade materials are used, because the light absorber–substrate combination optimized for PV applications is harnessed in the photoelectrode fabrication as well (e.g. soda lime glass/Mo for CIGS and sapphire or Ge for III/V, etc.). However, the substrate is an important price factor of the whole PEC cells and efficiency loss (by ohmic loss) from the substrate is significant. Since a photoelectrode always comes with a substrate of equivalent size to the device, a large device needs a large substrate accompanied by high price and high ohmic loss.
Holmes-Gentle et al. estimated the loss by the FTO substrate for selected model photoelectrodes.196 Ohm's law (J = σE) and the continuity of charge were used in order to simulate the electric currents through a conductive medium under an electric field. Here, J is the current density, σ the conductivity (=1/resistance), E the electric field, and Φ the electric potential. The term Q is the current sources/sinks within the medium. Loss can be numerically calculated from the formula ∇J = Q and E = −∇Φ. The analysis assumes 2D geometry of σ since the thickness ranges from 150 nm to 10 μm, and thus the major σ factor would be TCO itself (2.2 × 10−6 Ω m−1). Fig. 33 shows that a large size (L = 5–50 cm) gives a large potential drop for the same photoelectrode. For all model photoelectrodes, efficiency achieved on the laboratory scale would decrease to nearly 15% for a 50 cm device losing nearly 80% of current density. Thus such a potential drop is indeed a serious problem.
 Fig. 33  Potential distribution across the substrate for model photoelectrodes. (a) Color map of substrate potential overlaid with model equations and boundary conditions, for U0 = 1.23 VRHE and L = 10 cm. (b) Potential distribution at y = L/2 for a boundary condition U0 = 1.23 VRHE for various electrode sizes. (c) Effective current density results for various electrode sizes using model photoelectrode datasets. (d) Current losses for various electrode widths for model photoelectrode datasets. Reproduced from ref. 196 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. 
An easy way to reduce ohmic loss is to use metallic grids on a substrate.256 Recently, Hankin et al.195 proposed an interesting substrate design to overcome such a problem for Sn:Fe2O3 on an optimized Ti metal substrate. Thus, a modestly large Fe2O3 photoanode (10 × 10 cm2) prepared by spray pyrolysis on a Ti substrate showed 250 mA/100 cm2 at 1.6 VRHE, which is one of the highest current density values for such a large scale photoelectrode. Calculation of potential drop for a flat or perforated Ti substrate was conducted using the COMSOL Multiphysics 4.4 program. As shown in Fig. 34, the perforated substrate has a lower potential gradient not only from the photoanode to the Pt cathode but also at the photoanode itself. Unfortunately, better performance could not be achieved in three electrode analysis (Fig. 34d), which requires further optimization of the system. The total device, assuming two back to back photoanodes/cathodes, was also calculated to show the significant current density drop from 2.5 mA cm−2 (at edge point of photoanode/cathode) to 1.0 mA cm−2 (for longest L = 100 cm). This work not only emphasizes the importance of substrate design but also cell architecture for minimizing such potential and current drop.172
 Fig. 34  (a) Electrode geometries for analysis in a PEC reactor: (a1) directly illuminated photoanode surface (Fe2O3 film on a Ti foil) facing away from the counter electrode; (a2) directly illuminated photoanode surface (hematite film on a perforated Ti foil substrate) facing away from the counter electrode. (b) Solid and perforated 100 cm2 Ti substrates for Sn:Fe2O3 photoanodes fabricated by spray pyrolysis. (c) Secondary current density distribution at the illuminated solid (c1) and perforated (c2) Sn:Fe2O3 photoanode surfaces in 1 M NaOH electrolyte as a function of spatial coordinates in a PEC reactor at 1.5 VRHE. (d) Effects of potential and electrode structure on experimental and predicted current densities for 100 cm2 Sn:Fe2O3/Ti in 1 M NaOH solution. (e and f) Predicted local current density distribution of Pt cathode – Sn:Fe2O3/Ti photo-bipole under a Xe arc lamp of 312 W m2 and 0.7 VSHE (1.5 VRHE). Reproduced from ref. 195 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. 
4.6 Stability
As discussed above, there are many semiconductor materials (especially PV-grade materials) that show ηSTH way above 10%. But in practice, a more important question is whether such inorganic light absorbers are stable in aqueous electrolytes under irradiation. A highly efficient, low band gap material that is also stable at the same time is yet to be found. For photoanode materials, it is very common that destructive oxidation of the material itself by holes (e.g. CdS + 2h+ → Cd2+ + S) occurs instead of oxidation of water. A material's inherent chemical stability can be predicted from its own oxidation/reduction potential as illustrated in Fig. 35. Thus, when photoelectrons/holes can move to reduction/oxidation potentials, the material becomes unstable due to electron/hole attacks. These routes are blocked for metal oxides like the first case of Fig. 35a and stable for both HER and OER.208 Other non-oxide materials allow either or both of these attacks. This instability problem of non-oxide materials could be mitigated by using a protection layer and efficient co-catalysts like Ni/TiO2/p–n Si or Ni/TiO2/p–n GaAs.175 But the protection measures increases the complexity of the photoelectrode, which will increase the capital cost of the solar hydrogen production device.16
 Fig. 35  (a) Chemical stability of a photoelectrode without any bias in view of thermodynamics. Reproduced from ref. 257 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2013. (b) Calculated oxidation (red bars) and reduction (black bars) potentials, HER/OER potentials with NHE (pH 0), and band gaps of various photocatalysts. Reproduced from ref. 208 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2012. 
As shown in Fig. 36a, stability is one of the most important factors for lowering the price of produced hydrogen. Stability determines the life span of the light absorber, and varying it from 5 to 20 years makes the expected ERORI nearly 5 times different.28,29 System analysis showed that at least 10 years of lifetime are needed to have a positive outlook for the system to be practical.30,198 The stability issue on the experimental bench scale is well addressed in a review by Bae et al.48 The longest stability test for ∼100 days was reported,48 but those tests over 10 days used materials protected with relatively expensive methods like ALD and sputtering, which would face scalability problems. In addition, the stability up to 100 days would not guarantee the stability during the expected life span of the devices of more than 10 years.29,30,40
 Fig. 36  Bench scale stability test of photoelectrodes. (a) Cost of H2 produced by a PEC device with 10% efficiency as a function of panel lifetime and cost. The calculations include only capital costs, maintenance and process control costs. Reproduced from ref. 15 with permission from the IFP Energies nouvelles, copyright 2015. (b) 1100 h discontinuous water splitting with a NiFeOx/Mo:BiVO4/Ni/Sn electrode at 0.6 VRHE in KBi buffer (pH ∼ 9.2). Reproduced from ref. 114 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2016. (c) Stability test at a fixed bias of 0.5VRHE with chopped illumination for a RuOx/TiO2/Ga2O3/Cu2O photocathode in pH 5 electrolyte. Reproduced from ref. 140 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2018. (d) Stability of a CoOx/SiOx/n-Si photoanode held at 1.63 VRHE under 1.1 Sun in 1.0 M KOH. Reproduced from ref. 259 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2016. (e) A device architecture of a c-Si based photocathode. HR-TEM shows the carrier-selective contact (c-Si/SiOx/nc-Si). Long-term stability test of the c-Si photocathode with a TiO2 protection layer at an applied bias of 0.4 VRHE in 1 M HClO4 (pH 0). Reproduced from ref. 260 with permission from Elsevier B.V., copyright 2017. 

Fig. 36b–e shows examples of long term stability tests reported for photoelectrodes. Metal oxides like TiO2, WO3, BiVO4 are reported to have stability up to 10–100 h in proper electrolytes258 and recently Kuang et al. reported 1100 h for NiFeOx/Mo:BiVO4/Ni/Sn photoanodes under a slightly basic condition (pH 9.2).114 A relatively high current density of up to 3.0 mA cm−2 was obtained, but the used fabrication method was not fully solution-based. The work also emphasized the importance of anions in the electrolyte, which greatly affected the stability of the photoanode. As mentioned above, Lee and Choi211 fabricated all solution-based photoanode–NiOOH/FeOOH/N2:BiVO4/FTO in vanadium-dissolved electrolyte under a slightly basic condition (pH 9.2) to achieve ∼500 h of stability. But this also requires dissolution of vanadium, which increases the cost of operation. Fe2O3 was also reported with the highest stability of ∼1000 h187 under alkaline conditions (pH 13.6), reported by the PECDEMO project.255 However, all of these recently achieved results indicate meaningful progress toward photoelectrodes stable over the scale of years.
The stability problem is more serious in photocathode materials. Hence, the majority of photocathodes come with protection and charge collecting layers between semiconductor||electrolyte interfaces. For example, RuOx/TiO2/Ga2O3/Cu2O achieved nearly 100 h of stability, which is a significantly improved record from its first demonstration.138 As mentioned, Lewis's group successfully demonstrated ∼1000 h scale stability for both photoanodes and photocathodes made from PV grade materials.131,134,175,259 A TiO2 protected, Pt electrocatalyzed Si photocathode achieved stability of up to 40 days in a highly acidic electrolyte.260 In spite of these successful demonstrations of adequate stability of the protected photocathodes, their practical application in year-scale operation has to be investigated further because exposure of the materials to electrolytes by cracks or pin holes of the protection layer would kill the device almost instantly. In addition, the protective layers, such as Al2O3 or TiO2, are fabricated employing sophisticated and expensive methods instead of cheap solution-based methods.
4.7 Fuel separation and membranes
Fuel separation and transfer is an important factor in practical large scale solar hydrogen production, but has been rarely studied. Fuel separation is mostly accomplished by a membrane electrolyte.172 In laboratory investigations, the performance of the membrane does not affect the overall performance of the device because of the short proton exchange pathway. Of course, this pathway becomes much longer as the device size increases and then the membrane performance becomes much more important. A simple porous ceramic brings gas crossover and unwanted back reactions, as well as possible safety issues. There are two types of ionic membrane; cation-exchange membrane (CEM, e.g. Nafion) and anion-exchange membrane (AEM, e.g. Selemion). The expected duration of the membrane's functional time should be counted as an operational factor, since the price for membrane and fuel separation is far from negligible (for usual planar type, nearly 20% of the light absorber cost).14,29
The importance of membranes was investigated by Jin et al.261 through experiments and simulation. Thus, a “membrane-less” system showed a significant pH gradient and a high crossover of H2 gas (40% in O2 chamber!) as shown in Fig. 37a. Thus, a membrane or at least a similar system is needed especially for the case of high current density and a large amount of fuel like a practical large system.
 Fig. 37  (a) Devices with and without membranes (a1). An integrated, membrane-free solar water-splitting device (a2). Cross-sectional pH profiles in the membrane-free device (a3). Reproduced from ref. 261 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. (b) A CoP/Ti mesh cathode in H2SO4 (pH = 0) and a Ni/TiO2/InGaP/GaAs photoanode in KBi (pH 9.3) with a bipolar membrane (BPM) made of anion and cation exchange membranes (b1). Stability of an unbiased, two-electrode configuration (b2). (b3) Electrochemical potentials in the KBi (pH 9.3)/Fumasep® FBM/H2SO4 (pH 0) cell. (b4) Simulated pH profile across the BPM. Reproduced from ref. 263 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2016. 
Usually, OER favors an alkaline electrolyte, while HER favors acidic medium. A way to solve the electrolyte discrepancy problem in the same PEC cell is a bipolar membrane (BPM) made of double-sided two different types of membrane, which sustains different pH of two different electrolytes. Luo et al.99 showed a 2p PSC based PV–EC system operating with CoP HEC in pH 0 while NiFe LDH in pH 14, which are optimum pH for individual components. Similar work done by Jin et al.261 with a CoP/Ti mesh cathode in H2SO4 (pH = 0) and a photoanode of Ni/TiO2/InGaP/GaAs in KBi (pH 9.3) with BPM demonstrated significant stability of ∼100 h (Fig. 37b). Calculation showed a large pH gradient inside of the membrane.
Efforts are also underway to avoid the usage of membranes by using a clever geometry of the system. Recently, Landman et al.262 demonstrated that an auxiliary electrode NiOOH/Ni(OH)2-based capacitor connecting two water electrolysis cells could be operated without a membrane. Powered by Si PV, two different reactors without any membrane achieved an ηSTH of 7.5%.
4.8 Cell architecture design
Design of cell architecture of scaled-up devices plays a huge role in the output of the system and involved BOS.29,30,172 Cell architecture determines the balance of individual components, their integration and operating conditions. Simulation is an effective approach of cell architecture design reflecting the multi-dimensional (3D) configuration of the cell and mass transport, which become more important when device dimensions increase.15 The topic is well discussed by Lewis and coworkers.14,172 Many studies commonly indicate that completely different geometry and component assembly would be needed for PEC cells and PV–EC systems.172,198 We would like to discuss currently proposed and actually realized cell architectures of PEC cells (at least one light absorber is immersed in electrolyte) based on schemes shown in Fig. 38.
 Fig. 38  Proposed cell architectures of PEC cells for overall water splitting. (a) Planar panel with back to back stack. (b) Planar panel with side-by-side geometry. (c) Planar panels with tilted geometry, facing the membrane separator. (d) Reflection scheme for the 2nd light absorber. (e and f) Solar concentrator-coupled PEC cell with a focus light concentrator266 and a reflection concentrator. Architectures are drawn based on Xiang et al.'s164 arrangements. Reproduced from ref. 172 and 266 with permission from Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim, copyright 2016. 

Planar panel design. 
In this most common and simplest design, light absorbers are piled up back to back with an internal charge collector. The overall geometry resembles a solar panel dipped in an electrolyte. This geometry, however, requires a good transparency of the two light absorbers and substrates, which would limit the material selection. In particular, p-type materials use a hole transfer layer of low transparency, e.g. CIGS-based photocathodes often use opaque Mo/SLG as a substrate.141,154,178,264 An alternative is a “cappuccino cell”, in which front and back light absorbers face each other and only the front absorber is supposed to be transparent. But this also brings a greater issue for anion/cation exchange since the membrane is between the two light absorbers. Proper selection and design of membrane is needed to minimize the pH gradient. For example, a recent study about BiVO4 (pH 7) with a bipolar membrane and a cathode in different pH (pH 0) showed a moderate current density of 2.0 mA cm−2 at 1.23 VRHE without significant potential gradient loaded to the PEC cell and showed the importance of membranes in such a planar design.265 Xiang et al.197,261,266 implemented a “tilted” design to compensate for this problem at least in part. As mentioned, many studies are of the opinion that PEC devices need their unique geometry rather than traditional planar solar panels because of the necessity of fuel separation and chemical gradient compensation.


Reflection. 
Pihosh et al.163 proposed that a photoanode facing up and a photocathode or a PV module at 90 degrees to the cell (Fig. 38d) can get reflected light from the photoanode. The position of the membrane was not discussed but it can be spatially separated for the case of PV as the bottom light absorber (HER in the dark). This scheme has less stringent material requirement for the front light absorber since transparency is not important.


Light concentrator. 
Simulation studies about PEC cells for scale-up showed that the light absorber is the key factor for the overall system's energy balance and expected benefits.40,266 Naturally, reducing the size of the light absorber is the easiest way to reduce the cost. For example, usage of a light concentrator, with 10 times concentrated light, significantly reduced the expected price of hydrogen compared to the planar device.


Vapor feed of water. 
Vapor feed of water as the reactant may present an advantage for materials and cell operation. For PV–EC, it was already proven that the reaction scale of ∼10 mA cm−2 was viable for the vapor feed system, which had significantly better stability and provided the possibility of using sea water, which would make scaled up device operation greatly cheaper. But the system requires a very different material configuration, reactant permeable membrane||photoelectrode (for PV–EC, electrocatalyst) interface should be well suited, and mass transfer should have much greater dominance. An integrated PEC cell sustained by water vapor was simulated and studied using a multi-physics numerical model197

5. Dissemination of solar hydrogen production systems
In this section, proposed dissemination scenarios of scaled up H2 production systems are discussed. Among the three solar H2 production systems, PV–EC is a mature and highly efficient technology that has already been applied to solar H2 refueling stations, but PEC and PC water splitting have never been used on a practical scale.
In Fig. 39, three scenarios for production and application of solar H2 are analyzed:255 hydrogen refueling station, industrial process applications and single home applications with nominal productivities of ∼400 kg per day, ∼4000 kg per day, and ∼1 kg per day, respectively. The H2 refueling station offers H2 at 810 bar to fuel the fuel cell vehicles (FCV), which carry a pressurized hydrogen tank. The introduced industrial process considers H2 at 20 bars for the utilization as feedstock for further processing. Finally, the single home application refers to a decentralized approach of solar H2 production and subsequent use for fuel cells to provide electric power needed in small buildings.255 These operations could be done in centralized and decentralized manners.
 Fig. 39  Solar hydrogen production and overview about potential utilization. (a) A hydrogen production and application scenario. Reproduced from ref. 255. PECDEMO is funded by the European Fuel Cell and Hydrogen Joint Undertaking (FCH-JU) (DOI: http://www.fch.europa.eu/project/photoelectrochemical-demonstrator-device-solar-hydrogen-generation). (b) A vision of a sustainable hydrogen fuel community based on artificial photosynthesis. Hydrogen is produced from a solar water-splitting power plant using seawater on floating ports, tankers and seashore plants. Reproduced from ref. 268 with permission from Macmillan Publishers Limited, part of Springer Nature, copyright 2012. (c) A personalized energy system. Reproduced from ref. 269 and 270 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2009. (d) A solar refinery for supporting carbon dioxide conversion to fuel. Reproduced from ref. 267 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. 
Centralized operation of solar H2 production considers reduced land price and better maintenance for centralized facilities and fuel distribution located in areas of high intensity solar illumination. Thus, centralized solar H2 production would have a similar grid system to PV technology. Thus, the Desertec Initiative proposed a network of concentrated solar and other renewable power plants in Northern Africa and around the Mediterranean region, where solar illumination of high intensity and low seasonal variation is available.15 There is even 1600 km of H2 pipelines already installed in Europe. On top of utilization as fuel for FCV, H2 can also be used for hydrogenation of CO2 to produce hydrocarbons, which also has fuel values. The hydrocarbons are easier for transportation than H2. Large capacity plants are required for centralized application such as large-scale solar H2 plants or solar refinery for solar fuel and chemical production by mimicking natural photosynthesis using CO2 produced from traditional power plants (Fig. 39a).267
Maeda and Domen52 also provided a perspective about solar hydrogen plants to provide a third of energy demand in 2050 as shown in Fig. 40. Estimation showed about 250000 km2 area of solar plant with an ηSTH of 10%. With an individual plant of 5 km × 5 km, 10000 of them would be needed. The 250000 km2 area corresponds to ∼1% of the earth's desert area and produces 570 ton of H2 gas per day.
 Fig. 40  Large-scale H2 production via solar water splitting. Reproduced from ref. 52 and 70 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2010. 
In spite of the advantages of the centralized solar H2 production facility, the intrinsic merits of solar energy harvesting and storage lie in de-centralized, small capacity systems. Small capacity systems are suitable for residential use (single home application) and storage systems, as in Fig. 39d, whose main elements are rooftop solar energy panels to produce electricity for heating, cooking, lighting, and charging the batteries of the owner's electric cars. An electrolyzer using excess power can convert water into H2 for storage and as a fuel for a residential fuel cell.269,270 Ronge et al.15 also remarked that only ∼8.6% of all urbanized areas are enough for solar panels to meet the world's demand of energy. The decentralized H2 production also mitigates transportation issues of H2 gas.271
Another good example of a decentralized solar H2 production facility is the H2 refueling station for FCV. Fig. 41 shows a conceptual design of solar H2 station that could work around the clock. During the day, sunlight is used to produce solar H2 that is directly used for FCV, and electricity generation by fuel cells and the rest is stored. At night, H2 stored during the day is used for FCV and electricity generation.
 Fig. 41  Use of solar energy around the clock. Capture of sunlight to produce fuel, storage, usage as solar fuel from hydrogen station and electricity generation. Reproduced from ref. 272 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, Copyright 2012. 
Utilization of solar energy on the large scale requires a huge amount of land and competition for space limits the scale up of solar H2 production.199 When land is limited, sea could be used instead for solar H2 production. Floating solar farms for electricity production (Fig. 42) have already been realized in China, Japan, United Kingdom, India and recently Singapore.273,274 Since the majority of the earth's surface is occupied by sea (∼70%), centralized solar H2 production on the sea could be a viable option. Since all solar H2 production needs water, use of sea water as an electrolyte would have great cost advantage. Recent development of electrocatalysts174 and photoelectrodes243 working properly in sea water (pH 8.0) instead of usual electrolytes suggests high promise of using sea water as a potential electrolyte. The device could perform water electrolysis without pumps or membranes and could lead to low-cost sustainable hydrogen production.
 Fig. 42  Solar farms installed on (a) land, Australia (Nyngan Solar Farm). Reproduced with permission from ref. 276 and (b) lake, Singapore, the world's largest floating solar photovoltaic cell. Reproduced from ref. 273 with permission of The Straits Times, copyright 2016. 
A photovoltaic-powered electrolysis device was developed that can operate as a stand-alone platform that floats on open water described as a “solar fuels rig”.275 The device relies on an electrode configuration that allows the gases to be separated and collected using the buoyancy of bubbles in water. The prototype is the first demonstration of a practical membrane-less floating PV–electrolyzer system, and could inspire large-scale ‘solar fuels rigs’ that could generate large quantities of H2 fuel from abundant sunlight and seawater without taking up any space on land or competing with fresh water for agricultural uses.
6. Large-scale demonstrations of PEC water splitting
For solar H2 production, PV–EC has already been demonstrated on large scales. It is rare to find actual demonstration of large scale PEC cells. But owing to the rapid development, in the past decade, of the laboratory scale PEC water splitting technology, there are small but increasing numbers of PEC demonstration projects that we like to introduce in this section. These case studies are invaluable trials of field operation at this early stage of technology development and have a goal to implement prototypes to test the techno-economic feasibility of practical larger commercial scale PEC water splitting plants or solar H2 farms.
As mentioned already, the first barrier of the scale up is that the bigger photoelectrode gives greatly reduced overall efficiency owing to the sheet resistance of the substrate. The substrate with a conductive grid reduces the efficiency loss as practiced for solar cells. Lee et al.256 demonstrated WO3/FTO films up to size of ∼130 cm2 by screen printing of WO3 nanoparticle paste synthesized by sol–gel precipitation (Fig. 43a). Enlarged WO3/FTO suffered great performance decrement – Jph at 1.23 VRHE decreased from 2.63 mA cm−2 to 1.5 mA cm−2 to 1.18 mA cm−2 for photoanode sizes of 0.36, 4.8 and 130.6 cm2. In the meantime, an Ag grid embedded in the film was greatly beneficial – for the 4.8 cm2 photoanode, nearly 130% enhanced photocurrent density was observed relative to a photoanode of the same size with no Ag grid. With the optimized Ag grid-applied WO3 photoanode, a tandem cell of WO3-DSSC achieved a high photocurrent of 150 mA/130.56 cm2 (ηSTH of 1.41%). It is much lower than the laboratory scale ηSTH of 3.24% and 2% for 0.36 cm2 and 4.8 cm2 WO3 photoanodes, but the efficiency is quite high relative to other reports with similarly sized photoanodes. Under 1 sun, the tandem cell of the large area photoanode (130.56 cm2) produced 3 mL min−1 of H2. This work demonstrates the importance of charge collector optimization to minimize efficiency loss of large scale devices.
 Fig. 43  Large scale WO3 photoanode-DSSC tandem cell. (a) Schematic diagram of small area 0.6 × 0.6 cm2; 0.6 × 3.8 cm2; and 9.6 × 13.6 cm2 WO3 photoanodes with Ag grid-embedded FTO as the substrate, coating of WO3 on the Ag grid-embedded FTO substrate, and Ag encapsulation using epoxy. (b) J–V curves of photoanodes in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte under 1 sun illumination. (c) Experimental PEC set-up with 130.56 cm2 WO3 photoanode, platinum counter electrode and calibrated cylindrical syringe for gas collection immersed in 0.5 M H2SO4. (d) Operating point of a scaled-up WO3 photoanode (130.56 cm2) and a DSSC module (front-side series, 3 cell connected). Reproduced from ref. 256 with permission from Elsevier B.V., copyright 2011. 
A full study of actual large scale PEC cell operation was conducted by Vilanova et al.,187 which nicely reflects the majority of issues for scale up of PEC cells that were discussed so far (Fig. 44). They designed a PEC cell with a planar cell architecture named a “CoolPEC cell” and observed fundamental factors related to the operation of PEC cells; (i) fluid dynamics for gas separation, (ii) temperature profile, and (iii) actual large sized Fe2O3/FTO photoanode (50 cm2) operating up to 1008 h, one of the longest successful operations of the photoanode with 0.45 mA cm−2 at 1.6 V, which was provided by a Si solar cell. First, a PEC cell was devised with a housing composed of a stainless steel frame and acrylic body, double side window used as the reactor, working electrode (Fe2O3/FTO as backside window), Pt loaded Ti as the cathode, and an ion exchange membrane. Computational fluid dynamics (CFD) and actual observation of electrolyte flow was studied to reduce bubble effects under gas generation corresponding to 5.4 mA cm−2, demonstrating that their CoolPEC cell showed negligible effect of light blocking by gas bubbles, which was a serious problem for large scale operation. The CoolPEC cell also maintained a moderate temperature of 45 °C without a significant gradient in the reactor with a constant flow rate of 0.5 L min−1 electrolyte feeding.
 Fig. 44  (a) CoolPEC cell. 1 – acrylic embodiment; 2 – front window (synthetic quartz); 3 – front stainless steel frame; 4 – internal acrylic plate dividing the WE compartment; 5 – main electrolyte inlet; 6 – external screw (current collector for the CE); 7 – back stainless steel frame; 8 – back window (photoelectrode); 9 – acrylic cap; 10 – electrolyte outlet; 11 – internal gasket; 12 – external gasket; 13 – platinized-Ti mesh; 14 – ion exchange membrane; 15 – acrylic holders; 16 and 17 – metal connectors between the Pt–Ti mesh and the external screw. (b) Assessment of light trapping due to bubble accumulation inside the PEC cell. 1 – simulated sunlight (1000 W m−2); 2 – front window (synthetic quartz); 3 – back window (FTO glass, 2.2 mm, 7 Ω sq); 4 – electrolyte outlet; 5 – electrolyte inlet; 6 – Si calibrator used to monitor the solar irradiance reaching the outer surface of the back window. Solar irradiance reaching the external surface of the back window was monitored over 4 min with the PEC cell operating at 5.4 mA cm−2 with 1.0 M KOH and a feeding flow rate of 0.5 L min−1. (c) Long-term stability of a hematite photoelectrode under a constant bias of 1.6 V and simulated solar irradiation (1000 W m−2). Reproduced from ref. 187 with permission from Elsevier B.V., copyright 2018. 
The above reactor design and study conducted by Vilanova et al.187 was well implemented in PECDEMO's demonstration of a large scale PEC cell.255 The project investigated a multitude of aspects of PEC cell scale up including development of photoelectrode materials, simulation of the cell and a large scale system. First, large sized photoelectrodes of BiVO4, Cu2O and Fe2O3 were fabricated. For the large area BiVO4 electrode (>50 cm2), Mo metal grids were deposited on FTO glass to improve the charge collection efficiency as shown in Fig. 45a. They performed 4 times of mosaic spray pyrolysis (3.6 × 3.6 cm2) with mask to obtain a modest current of ∼70 mA at a bias of 1.23 VRHE, which was much lower than that observed with a 1.0 cm2 scale device. The PECDEMO project group also designed a 4-stripe type Cu2O photocathode with metal grid lines (Fig. 45c). Two metal grids of Au and Ag showed a significant improvement in the performance of the large scale photocathode relative to a plane photocathode, again indicating the importance of the substrate design.
 Fig. 45  Large scale fabrication of photoelectrode conducted by PECDEMO. (a) Large area BiVO4 (7.1 × 7.1 cm2 excluding metal grids). (b) J–V curve of large area BiVO4 (1 sun, 0.1 M phosphate + 0.5 M Na2SO3). (c) Large area Cu2O photocathode with grid lines (7.1 cm × 7.1 cm = 50.4 cm2 active area). (d) Linear sweep voltammetry scans of large area Cu2O photocathode for the vertical PEC cell under chopped illumination (0.6 sun) in the pH 5 electrolyte. Reproduced from ref. 255. PECDEMO is funded by the European Fuel Cell and Hydrogen Joint Undertaking (FCH-JU) (DOI: http://www.fch.europa.eu/project/photoelectrochemical-demonstrator-device-solar-hydrogen-generation). 
The large area (50 cm2) photoanodes showed a bit lower performance than laboratory scale (∼1.0 cm2) as expected, but a more serious problem was the instability of BiVO4. Etching of BiVO4 on FTO was observed visually, which is an indicator of corrosion. But when the W:BiVO4 photoanode with Co-Pi as OEC in 0.1 M KPi as electrolyte was replaced with a Co-Ci/W:BiVO4 photoanode, near 70–100 h of stability in bicarbonate electrolyte was observed, emphasizing the importance of good OEC and electrolyte. Moderate stability with the same OEC and electrolyte combination was also reported by Kim et al.166 on a laboratory scale.
Tandem cells of a BiVO4-heterojunction with intrinsic thin layer (HIT) Si PV or Fe2O3–HIT Si PV were tested under outdoor sunlight using a planar “vertical” or solar concentrator based on angled illumination as shown in Fig. 46. The vertical device tested at the test facility SoCRatus of German Aerospace Center (DLR) showed maximum unbiased current density of 1.88 mA cm−2 (ηSTH =2.3%) for BiVO4–HIT Si PV and 0.52 mA cm−2 (ηSTH = 0.74%) for Fe2O3–HIT Si PV.
For the “angled” device tested at UPorto, actual illumination was much larger than the size of the device (∼200 cm2) since a reflection concentrator was used (Fig. 46a and b). Operation was conducted for 15–48 h with no significant degradation of efficiency. Unfortunately, the efficiency observed in laboratory for the 50 cm2 device could not be achieved. Thus, the BiVO4–HIT Si PV tandem cell showed an ηSTH of 0.2% and the Fe2O3–HIT Si PV tandem cell only 0.058% for only the “angled” device (Fig. 46e and f). Even though the light concentrator-based system was expected to achieve much better system operation from system assessment and actual observation for III/V light absorbers, this was not the case here. Indeed, numerous factors exist for scale up of PEC cells, including very large intermittence of illumination. In Fig. 46f, for instance, the variation of ηSTH ranges from 0.42% to 0.12% for 8 days, or by a factor of almost 4.
 Fig. 46  Demonstration of a PEC cell with a solar concentrator based on angled illumination. (a) DLR's test facility SoCRatus with its main components. (b) Prototype module in acrylic embodiment composed of four individual 50 cm2 PEC cells (1 × 4 array, total active area of 200 cm2) mounted in the focal plane of the SoCRatus (here without reflective shields). (c) Modular prototype with Fe2O3 and (d) BiVO4 photoelectrodes. (e) Total irradiation on the prototype (smoothed ± 30 s), average current density (smoothed ± 30 s), and hydrogen flow relative to the respective mean values as well as average ηSTH without and with bias voltage of the particular days associated with Fe2O3 photoelectrodes. Gray field refers to test of single compartments. (f) Total irradiation on the prototype (smoothed ± 30 s), average current density (smoothed ± 30 s), and hydrogen flow relative to the respective mean values as well as average ηSTH of the particular days associated with BiVO4 photoelectrodes. The ηSTH achieved from the device is marked on the upper side of the graph. Reproduced from ref. 255. PECDEMO is funded by the European Fuel Cell and Hydrogen Joint Undertaking (FCH-JU) (DOI: http://www.fch.europa.eu/project/photoelectrochemical-demonstrator-device-solar-hydrogen-generation). 
Tolod et al.277 used the Artiphyction project to scale up BiVO4-based photoelectrodes with PEC–PV tandem cells of 1.6 m2 photoelectrodes (Fig. 47), which they described as the TRL5 development. Many smaller 64 cm2 size modules of the BiVO4-Si tandem cell achieved an ηSTH of 3%, which fell to 2% during the long-term operation due to multiple factors such as bubble formation in the reactor. As mentioned, bubble coverage blocks light and mass transfer becomes sluggish.
 Fig. 47  (a) Progress of large scale PEC cell devices from the Artiphyction prototype. (b) Photograph and close view of the 1.6 m2 Artiphyction prototype made of 100 PEC cell modules (8 × 8 cm2 of Co-Pi/BiVO4/FTO window and Co nanoparticle as cathode). (c) J–V plots for a single PEC window (thus unit is mA/64 cm2). Reproduced from ref. 189 with permission from the MDPI AG (Basel, Switzerland) and Professor Guido Saraco and Professor Simelys Hernandez, copyright 2017. 
7. Solar hydrogen refueling stations
The first commercial solar hydrogen production will find its application to solar hydrogen refueling stations. So far, solar H2 stations constructed have employed PV–EC systems as summarized in Table 4. Typically, they are made of PV panels, an electrolyzer and a separate compressor unit to create high-pressure hydrogen to fuel FCV.

Honda conducted a development program towards a solar H2 powered society of the future by studying the entire process of solar H2 production, storage and supply, including the investigation of technologies that would reduce the level of carbon dioxide. Honda's next-generation solar hydrogen station (SHS) reduces the size of the system, while producing enough H2 (0.5 kg) through an 8 h overnight fill for daily commuting (16000 km per year) for a fuel cell vehicle. Compatible with a smart grid energy system, Honda's SHS would enable users to refill their vehicle overnight without the requirement for hydrogen storage. During daytime peak power times, SHS can export renewable electricity to the grid, providing a cost benefit to the customer. Engineered for slow, overnight refilling of a FCV, the home-use SHS can replenish the H2 required for typical daily driving and commuting. The home station was also designed to complement a public network of fast-fill hydrogen stations. The General Motor's Chevrolet fueling system consists of two subsystems: (i) a set of solar arrays, and (ii) an electrolyzer, storage, and dispensing system (ESD).278
About 100 H2 fueling stations exist in the United States, most of which are in private ownership by companies. Los Angeles hosts 8 public fueling stations, and 100 more are planned in California by 2024. In Europe, Germany is at the forefront of public H2 fueling stations. The current number of ca. 20 is to be augmented to 400 by 2023. A solar-driven H2 station was built in Freiburg, which produces its own H2 using solar energy captured by PV panels on the roof. Almost 20 such stations exist in Germany and USA. Nanoptek developed a water splitting device that combines an integrated photocatalyst with external PV panels. One of the main energy applications of H2 today remains its use in forklifts in distribution centers. Over 6200 such forklifts are in operation in the US, used by Walmart, Sysco, Central Grocers and BMW. Colruyt, a Belgian retailer, together with WaterstofNet and Hydrogenics installed a H2 fueling station that is powered by their own excess wind and solar energy. Another success story of H2 was found in public transportation, with dozens of H2 fueled buses involved in pilot projects in cities such as London, Beijing, Berlin and Perth.15 Different hydrogen stations available in the world are summarized with their capacity, in Table 4.
8. Summary and outlook
Solar overall water splitting has been widely and intensively studied for the past 50 years using photovoltaic, photocatalyst and photoelectrode-driven systems. These technologies achieved significant improvements during the last 10 years reaching solar-to-hydrogen conversion efficiencies ηSTH of nearly 2% (PC), 3% (PE–PE), 8% (PEC–PV hybrid), 19% (buried junction PV) and 30% (PV–EC with PEM electrolyzer), and are still rapidly under progress for under-developed PC and PEC technologies. A technology map is plotted in Fig. 48 for efficiency vs. system complexity. The trend is quite clear; intrinsically stable metal oxide materials in general have low efficiencies and low complexity. The PV-grade materials exhibit high efficiencies, but their higher complexity means higher cost. Thus, efficiency is one of the many considerations to make in the selection of solar hydrogen production technology.
 Fig. 48  Technological map showing various photon-driven water splitting approaches for solar energy conversion and specific experimental demonstrations. Presentation format followed that of Sayama et al.280 with permission from the National Institute of Advanced Industrial Science and Technology, copyright 2014. 
With achievements in enhancing ηSTH to approach the target value of at least 10%, attention has been paid to the scale up of the solar hydrogen production technologies. In this aspect, the PV–EC technology is way ahead of PC or PEC technologies because many solar hydrogen refueling stations are under commercial operation in many parts of the world. Yet, there are several demonstration projects ongoing in order to scale up PEC solar hydrogen production systems. So far, demonstrations of PEC cells have been done only around 1 m2 scale, but many expected problems and challenges have already been identified in scaling up of materials themselves, balance of systems, and operation issues for serious consideration. The results of those scale-up projects and techno-economic assessments indicate that, at the current level of development, PEC water splitting cannot compete with PV–EC devices. To make the PEC cells competitive, their ηSTH should go above 10%, and lifetime longer than 10 years. This suggests that material developments have to go in parallel with the scale up and demonstration projects for practical application of PEC hydrogen production technology on a large scale.

Table Content:

Jin Hyun Kim
	Jin Hyun Kim received his undergraduate degree from the University of Seoul, Republic of Korea, in 2012 and joined Prof. Jae Sung Lee's research group as an integrated MS + PhD candidate of Pohang University of Science and Technology (POSTECH), Republic of Korea. He received his PhD on the subject of semiconductor photoelectrode development for photoelectrochemical solar energy conversion in 2016. He is now working as a postdoctoral fellow in Jae Sung Lee's group (2012–2018) at Ulsan National Institute of Science and Technology (UNIST) to undertake a project to develop metal oxide-based solar energy harvesting, conversion, and storage.

Dharmesh Hansora
	Dharmesh Hansora is pursuing his PhD degree (2017 onwards) under the guidance of Prof. Jae Sung Lee at the School of Energy and Chemical Engineering, UNIST, South Korea. He received his Bachelor's and Master's degree in Chemical Engineering from MS University of Baroda, India. His current research area includes benchmarking of devices and scale-up to large-scale systems for solar water splitting.

Pankaj Sharma
	Pankaj Sharma received his PhD degree in Chemistry from Jiwaji University, India, in 2008. He worked as a Senior Lecturer at Lovely Professional University, Punjab, and MM University, Haryana, India, from 2008–2009. In December 2009 he joined Myongji University, Republic of Korea, as a Research Professor. After that he worked at the Korea Institute of Energy Research as a Research Scientist (2010–2012), and then as a Research Professor at Chungnam National University (2012–2017), Republic of Korea. Presently, he is working as a Senior Research Scientist with Prof. Ji-Wook Jang at Ulsan National Institute of Science and Technology, Republic of Korea. His research interests include the synthesis of multifunctional, hybrid and nanomaterials for their applications in energy, environmental and biomedical fields.

Ji-Wook Jang
	Ji-Wook Jang studied Chemistry at Pohang University of Science and Technology (POSTECH), Republic of Korea, and obtained his PhD degree in Chemical Engineering from the same university in 2012 under the supervision of Prof. Jae Sung Lee. He continued extensive research on different types of photocatalysts, and photocatalytic systems while working at Boston College, USA, and Institute for Solar Fuels, Germany, under the guidance of Prof. Dunwei Wang, and Prof. Roel van de Krol, respectively. He is presently working as an Assistant Professor at the School of Energy and Chemical Engineering from 2016. His research interest includes the photochemical production of future energy carriers (H2, H2O2, NH3, etc.) and high valued chemicals.

Jae Sung Lee
	Jae Sung Lee received his BS degree from Seoul National University in 1975, MS degree from Korea Advanced Institute of Science and Technology in 1977, Korea, and PhD degree from Stanford University in 1984. After a brief tenure at Catalytica Inc. as a research fellow, he returned to his native Korea to become a professor of chemical engineering at Pohang University of Science and Technology. He and his group moved to Ulsan National Institute of Science and Technology in 2013. He has been leading a research group of the “eco-friendly catalysis and energy laboratory”, whose research interests are focused on photocatalysis for energy applications, electrocatalysis for fuel cells, and heterogeneous catalysis of CO2 utilization. In photocatalysis, his research focuses on the development of photocatalytic solid semiconductor materials for solar energy conversion using heterojunction, surface modification, doping and solid solution technologies.

Absorption of light: SC + hν (>Eg) → e−cb + h+vb
Water oxidation (OER): 2H2O + 4h+vb → O2 + 4H+	(1)
Proton reduction (HER): 2H + 2e−cb → H2	(2)
Overall water splitting: 2H2O → 2H2 + O2 ΔG° = 237.2 kJ mol−1	(3)

 	Fig. 2  Solar energy-driven water splitting using a light absorber. If a single light absorber is used, the energy of the photon (hν) should be greater than 1.23 eV. Otherwise, multiple light absorbers are needed to drive the overall water splitting. (a) Spectrum of AM 1.5 G (i.e. 1 sun condition, 100 mW cm−2 power density). (b) Photoelectrolysis of water is an energetically uphill reaction. Reproduced from ref. 18 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2009. (c) Photochemical redox reactions for overall water splitting driven by light of sufficient energy (hν > Eg) to excite electron/hole pairs. E(hν) is the voltage achievable from photons with an energy of hν and Eg is the voltage that the light absorber can achieve using photons. ηloss includes potential loss from the internal resistance (material's own mobility and charge transfer efficiency), quenching of the photon's energy, and overpotential for oxygen and hydrogen evolution reactions (ηOER and ηHER). (d) Technical challenges of photon-driven overall water splitting.

 	Fig. 3  (a) Single light absorber-2 electron system (S2) configuration. (b) Maximum theoretical ηSTH of the S2 configuration as a function of the bandgap of the photoabsorber. (c) Dual light absorber (D4) of side-by-side configuration. (d) Maximum theoretical ηSTH of side-by-side D4. (e) Stacked D4 configuration, (f) maximum theoretical ηSTH of stacked D4. Images (b, d and f) were reproduced from ref. 40 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2013.	 

 	Fig. 4  Various photo-driven water splitting systems and their charge flows upon light absorption; (a) PC, (b) PEC and (c) PV–EC systems. Typical performance data acquired by these systems are also shown – H2, O2 gas evolution from PC, current matching of the photoanode and photocathode for PEC, and current matching of the PV cell and electrocatalyst (OEC, HEC combined) for the PV–EC system. They also show how to determine the STH efficiency from each system: (a) PC system requires measuring H2 produced per illumination area and time (μmole cm−2 h−1). (b) PEC (photoanode–photocathode) system requires two electrode J–V curves, and the operating point is the photoanode–photocathode cell's short circuit current density, which corresponds to the current density at 0.0 V bias. (c) PV–EC system requires J–V curves of PV and EC, and operating point will be the PV–EC cell's short circuit current density. All data were assumed values for demonstration purposes. For systems like (b) and (c), faradaic efficiency of the reaction is usually confirmed before calculating ηSTH from the current density generated from the system.	 

 	Fig. 5  Types of PEC cells for water splitting. (a) Photoanode–HEC cathode, (b) photocathode–OEC anode, (c) photoanode–photocathode tandem cell, and (d) photoanode–photovoltaic (PEC–PV) tandem cell for unbiased overall water splitting. Graphs below each figure are examples of J–V curves and operating points achieved from the system. Max ABPE (applied bias to photocurrent efficiency = Jph × (1.23 V – applied bias)/P(100 mW cm−2, 1 sun condition)) indicates maximum power density achievable from the system. Description and denotation were reproduced from ref. 49 with permission from Elsevier B.V., copyright 2017.

(b) Selectivity improvement of the photocatalyst via application of a cocatalyst (HEC, OEC) for suppression of unproductive back reactions (HOR, ORR). Reproduced from ref. 51 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2017.
